Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
The Core Proof: Two Impossible States for Power
Sanusi presents the third proof with two branches against power over action and its opposite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari concept of power over both an action and its opposite is impossible in two rational scenarios: balanced motivations and preponderant motivations.

Core Idea
Power over an action fails in both balanced and preponderant motivations — only determinism remains.

The third proof targets the idea that a person has power to do an action or its opposite. By analyzing the two possible states of motivation, the argument shows that such power is inconceivable. Let's examine this logical dilemma step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice how the proof is structured as a dilemma: if power exists, it must be in one of two states.
2
Identify the two states
Recognize the two possible states: balanced motivations (istiwā' al-dawā'ī) or preponderance (rujḥān) of one side over the other.
These are the only logical possibilities for the state of motivation when an agent acts.
3
Understand the impossibility in balanced state
In balanced motivations, achieving preponderance would contradict the balance. Since the agent cannot cause preponderance while balance remains, power over the action cannot exist in this state.
The key point: preponderance (rujḥān) contradicts balance (istiwā'), so power to produce preponderance is impossible.
4
Understand the impossibility in preponderant state
In a state of preponderance, the preponderant side becomes necessary (wājib) and the outweighed side impossible (mumtaniʿ). Neither the necessary nor the impossible can be subject to power.
Thus, power over the action (or its opposite) cannot exist in this state either.
5
Conclude the proof
Since both possible states are impossible for power, the very notion of such power is impossible. This leads to the conclusion that determinism (jabr) is necessary.
The proof is a powerful example of kalam reasoning using logical impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة: إنه لو حصلت القدرة على الفعل، لحصلت إما حال استواء الدواعي إلى الفعل والترك، أو حال رجحان أحد الجانبين على الآخر. والقسمان باطلان. فالقول بحصول هذه القدرة: محال. وإنما قلنا: إنه يمتنع حصول المكنة حال استواء الداعي. وذلك لأن الرجحان يناقض الاستواء فحصول الرجحان حال حصول الاستواء محال، والمحال لا قدرة عليه. وإنما قلنا: إنه يمتنع حصول هذه المكنة حال حصول رجحان أحد الطرفين، لأن الراجح واجب، والمرجوح ممتنع على ما يأتي برهانه مراراً، والواجب والممتنع لا قدرة له عليه البتة. فإن قالوا: إنه حال الاستواء، يمكنه إيقاع الترجيح في الزمان الثاني منه. فنقول: هذا باطل من وجهين: الأول: إنه إما أن يكون المراد أن عند مجيء الزمان الثاني، يمكنه إيقاع الترجيح في ذلك الزمان، أو يكون المراد: أن في الزمان الأول يمكنه إيقاع الترجيح في الزمان الثاني. والأول باطل، لأن عند مجيء الزمان الثاني، إما أن يكون الحاصل استواء الدواعي، أو رجحان أحد الجانبين. وحينئذ يعود التقسيم الأول. والثاني أيضاً: باطل. لأن عند حصول الزمان الأول يمتنع حصول الزمان الثاني، لكن إيقاع الفعل في الزمان الثاني، مشروط بحضور الزمان الثاني، الذي هو ممتنع الحضور في الزمان الأول. والموقوف على الممتنع: ممتنع، فوجب أن يكون إيقاع الفعل في الزمان الثاني عند حضور الزمان الأول: ممتنعاً. والممتنع لا قدرة عليه. والثاني: إن على هذا التقدير، يكون إيقاع الفعل حاصلا في الزمان الأول. ويكون وقوع الفعل حاصلا في الزمان الثاني. فهذا يوجب أن يكون إيقاع الفعل مغايراً لوقوعه. فنقول: ذلك المغاير المسمى بالإيقاع إن لم يكن واقعاً بقدرة هذا الفاعل، وجب أن يكون الفعل الواقع بسبب هذا الإيقاع: غير واقع بهذا القادر. وإن كان واقعاً بقدرة هذا الفاعل وجب أن تكون قدرته متقدمة عليه. ثم الكلام فيه كما في الأول. وهذا يوجب أن يكون كل إيقاع، مسبوقاً بإيقاع آخر، إلى غير النهاية بحسب الزمان. وذلك يوجب أن تكون قدرة القادر، متقدمة على حصول الأثر، بأزمنة لا نهاية لها. وكل ذلك محال.
The third proof: If power over the action existed, it would exist either in a state of balanced motivations (istiwā' al-dawā'ī) toward the act and its opposite, or in a state of preponderance (rujḥān) of one side over the other. Both divisions are false. So the assertion of the existence of this power is impossible. We say: It is impossible for capability (makna) to exist in a state of balanced motivation, because preponderance contradicts balance; thus the occurrence of preponderance while balance exists is impossible, and the impossible cannot be subject to power. And we say: It is impossible for this capability to exist in a state of preponderance of one side, because the preponderant (al-rājiḥ) is necessary (wājib) and the outweighed (al-marjūḥ) is impossible (mumtaniʿ), as its proof will come repeatedly, and the necessary and the impossible are not subject to power at all. If they say: In the state of balance, he can bring about preponderance in the second moment from it. We say: This is false in two ways. First: Either it is meant that when the second moment arrives, he can bring about preponderance in that moment, or it is meant that in the first moment he can bring about preponderance in the second moment. The first is false, because when the second moment arrives, either balanced motivations or preponderance of one side occurs, and then the first division returns. The second is also false, because when the first moment occurs, the occurrence of the second moment is impossible, but the bringing about of the action in the second moment is conditional upon the presence of the second moment, which is impossible to be present in the first moment. That which is dependent on the impossible is impossible, so it follows that bringing about the action in the second moment at the presence of the first moment is impossible, and the impossible is not subject to power. Second: On this assumption, the bringing about (īqāʿ) of the action occurs in the first moment, and the actual occurrence (wuqūʿ) of the action occurs in the second moment. This necessitates that the bringing about of the action is different from its occurrence. We say: That different thing called 'bringing about' (īqāʿ), if it is not actual by the power of this agent, then the action occurring due to this bringing about must not be actual by this powerful being (qādir). But if it is actual by the power of this agent, then his power must precede it. Then the discussion about it is as in the first case. This necessitates that every bringing about is preceded by another bringing about, ad infinitum in time. And that necessitates that the power of the powerful being precedes the occurrence of the effect by an infinite number of times. All of that is impossible.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof (al-ḥujja al-thālitha) against the Ashʿarī notion of power (qudra) over both an action and its opposite (al-fiʿl wa al-tark). The argument proceeds as follows: If a person had power over a specific action, this power would exist either (1) when motivations (dawāʿī) for the action and its opposite are perfectly balanced (istiwāʾ), or (2) when one side is preponderant (rujḥān). Both cases are shown to be impossible. In the balanced case, preponderance would contradict balance, so power over preponderance cannot coexist with balance. In the preponderant case, the preponderant side becomes necessary (wājib) and the outweighed side impossible (mumtaniʿ), and necessary and impossible things are not subject to power. Thus power over the action cannot exist. The text then addresses an objection: perhaps in the balanced state the agent can postpone preponderance to a second moment. The refutation has two parts. First, if the agent can bring about preponderance only when the second moment arrives, the same dilemma repeats (balance or preponderance at that moment). If he can bring it about from the first moment, then the second moment is not yet present, so bringing about the action in it is impossible (since the condition—the second moment's presence—is absent). Second, if one distinguishes between 'bringing about' (īqāʿ) in the first moment and 'occurrence' (wuqūʿ) in the second, then the bringing about itself must be caused by power; this leads to an infinite regress of bringings-about, which is impossible. The conclusion is that power over an action is impossible, implying determinism (jabr).

Key Terms

الفعل
الترك
الدواعي
الاستواء
الرجحان
الواجب
الممتنع
المكنة
الجبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof denies any human agency or free will entirely.
Correction: The proof specifically denies the Ash'ari concept of power over both an action and its opposite simultaneously, not all forms of agency. It argues that given a state of preponderance, the action becomes necessary, implying determinism.

Checkpoint
Why is it impossible for power to exist in a state of balanced motivations?
A) Because balanced motivations never occur in reality.
B) Because preponderance contradicts balance, so achieving preponderance through power is impossible.
C) Because power requires a stronger motivation.
D) Because balanced motivations lead to indecision, preventing action.

Key Takeaway
The third proof shows that power over an action is impossible in both balanced and preponderant motivational states, leading to determinism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If this proof shows that you have no independent power over your actions, how will you change the way you plan your day, turning to Allah in every small decision?
?
Which area of your life do you still act as if you are the sole agent, and what concrete step will you take today to acknowledge that Allah alone creates your actions?
?
Knowing that your failures and successes are both from Allah, how will you let this truth transform your sense of guilt and pride before Him this week?

Causality and Created Things
advanced

Refutation of the Objection: Two Ways to Deny Postponed Preponderance
Sanusi refutes the objection of postponed preponderance with two arguments: a repeated dilemma and infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that preponderance can be postponed to a later moment is refuted using two logical arguments: a repeated dilemma and an infinite regress.

Core Idea
Postponing preponderance leads to either a repeated dilemma or an infinite regress, both impossible.

An objector might argue that even in balanced motivations, the agent can postpone the act of preponderance to a later moment. The author dismantles this with two incisive arguments, showing that such postponement is either incoherent or leads to an infinite chain of 'bringings-about'. Let's follow the refutation step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the passage from 'فإن قالوا' to the end. Notice that the response is divided into two main parts: the first refutation, and the second refutation.
2
Understand the first refutation: the dilemma repeats
The first refutation has two sub-parts: either the preponderance is brought about in the second moment, leading to the same dilemma, or it is brought about from the first moment for the second moment, which is impossible because the second moment is not present.
The key point: the condition for the action (presence of the second moment) is absent in the first moment.
3
Understand the second refutation: infinite regress
The second refutation distinguishes between 'bringing about' (īqāʿ) in the first moment and 'occurrence' (wuqūʿ) in the second. The bringing about itself must be caused by power, leading to an infinite series of bringings-about before the effect occurs.
This regress is impossible because it would require an infinite number of prior power acts.
4
Conclude the refutation
Both refutations show that the attempted escape through postponement fails. Thus, the original proof stands: power over the action is impossible.
This reinforces the deterministic conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة: إنه لو حصلت القدرة على الفعل، لحصلت إما حال استواء الدواعي إلى الفعل والترك، أو حال رجحان أحد الجانبين على الآخر. والقسمان باطلان. فالقول بحصول هذه القدرة: محال. وإنما قلنا: إنه يمتنع حصول المكنة حال استواء الداعي. وذلك لأن الرجحان يناقض الاستواء فحصول الرجحان حال حصول الاستواء محال، والمحال لا قدرة عليه. وإنما قلنا: إنه يمتنع حصول هذه المكنة حال حصول رجحان أحد الطرفين، لأن الراجح واجب، والمرجوح ممتنع على ما يأتي برهانه مراراً، والواجب والممتنع لا قدرة له عليه البتة. فإن قالوا: إنه حال الاستواء، يمكنه إيقاع الترجيح في الزمان الثاني منه. فنقول: هذا باطل من وجهين: الأول: إنه إما أن يكون المراد أن عند مجيء الزمان الثاني، يمكنه إيقاع الترجيح في ذلك الزمان، أو يكون المراد: أن في الزمان الأول يمكنه إيقاع الترجيح في الزمان الثاني. والأول باطل، لأن عند مجيء الزمان الثاني، إما أن يكون الحاصل استواء الدواعي، أو رجحان أحد الجانبين. وحينئذ يعود التقسيم الأول. والثاني أيضاً: باطل. لأن عند حصول الزمان الأول يمتنع حصول الزمان الثاني، لكن إيقاع الفعل في الزمان الثاني، مشروط بحضور الزمان الثاني، الذي هو ممتنع الحضور في الزمان الأول. والموقوف على الممتنع: ممتنع، فوجب أن يكون إيقاع الفعل في الزمان الثاني عند حضور الزمان الأول: ممتنعاً. والممتنع لا قدرة عليه. والثاني: إن على هذا التقدير، يكون إيقاع الفعل حاصلا في الزمان الأول. ويكون وقوع الفعل حاصلا في الزمان الثاني. فهذا يوجب أن يكون إيقاع الفعل مغايراً لوقوعه. فنقول: ذلك المغاير المسمى بالإيقاع إن لم يكن واقعاً بقدرة هذا الفاعل، وجب أن يكون الفعل الواقع بسبب هذا الإيقاع: غير واقع بهذا القادر. وإن كان واقعاً بقدرة هذا الفاعل وجب أن تكون قدرته متقدمة عليه. ثم الكلام فيه كما في الأول. وهذا يوجب أن يكون كل إيقاع، مسبوقاً بإيقاع آخر، إلى غير النهاية بحسب الزمان. وذلك يوجب أن تكون قدرة القادر، متقدمة على حصول الأثر، بأزمنة لا نهاية لها. وكل ذلك محال.
The third proof: If power over the action existed, it would exist either in a state of balanced motivations (istiwā' al-dawā'ī) toward the act and its opposite, or in a state of preponderance (rujḥān) of one side over the other. Both divisions are false. So the assertion of the existence of this power is impossible. We say: It is impossible for capability (makna) to exist in a state of balanced motivation, because preponderance contradicts balance; thus the occurrence of preponderance while balance exists is impossible, and the impossible cannot be subject to power. And we say: It is impossible for this capability to exist in a state of preponderance of one side, because the preponderant (al-rājiḥ) is necessary (wājib) and the outweighed (al-marjūḥ) is impossible (mumtaniʿ), as its proof will come repeatedly, and the necessary and the impossible are not subject to power at all. If they say: In the state of balance, he can bring about preponderance in the second moment from it. We say: This is false in two ways. First: Either it is meant that when the second moment arrives, he can bring about preponderance in that moment, or it is meant that in the first moment he can bring about preponderance in the second moment. The first is false, because when the second moment arrives, either balanced motivations or preponderance of one side occurs, and then the first division returns. The second is also false, because when the first moment occurs, the occurrence of the second moment is impossible, but the bringing about of the action in the second moment is conditional upon the presence of the second moment, which is impossible to be present in the first moment. That which is dependent on the impossible is impossible, so it follows that bringing about the action in the second moment at the presence of the first moment is impossible, and the impossible is not subject to power. Second: On this assumption, the bringing about (īqāʿ) of the action occurs in the first moment, and the actual occurrence (wuqūʿ) of the action occurs in the second moment. This necessitates that the bringing about of the action is different from its occurrence. We say: That different thing called 'bringing about' (īqāʿ), if it is not actual by the power of this agent, then the action occurring due to this bringing about must not be actual by this powerful being (qādir). But if it is actual by the power of this agent, then his power must precede it. Then the discussion about it is as in the first case. This necessitates that every bringing about is preceded by another bringing about, ad infinitum in time. And that necessitates that the power of the powerful being precedes the occurrence of the effect by an infinite number of times. All of that is impossible.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof (al-ḥujja al-thālitha) against the Ashʿarī notion of power (qudra) over both an action and its opposite (al-fiʿl wa al-tark). The argument proceeds as follows: If a person had power over a specific action, this power would exist either (1) when motivations (dawāʿī) for the action and its opposite are perfectly balanced (istiwāʾ), or (2) when one side is preponderant (rujḥān). Both cases are shown to be impossible. In the balanced case, preponderance would contradict balance, so power over preponderance cannot coexist with balance. In the preponderant case, the preponderant side becomes necessary (wājib) and the outweighed side impossible (mumtaniʿ), and necessary and impossible things are not subject to power. Thus power over the action cannot exist. The text then addresses an objection: perhaps in the balanced state the agent can postpone preponderance to a second moment. The refutation has two parts. First, if the agent can bring about preponderance only when the second moment arrives, the same dilemma repeats (balance or preponderance at that moment). If he can bring it about from the first moment, then the second moment is not yet present, so bringing about the action in it is impossible (since the condition—the second moment's presence—is absent). Second, if one distinguishes between 'bringing about' (īqāʿ) in the first moment and 'occurrence' (wuqūʿ) in the second, then the bringing about itself must be caused by power; this leads to an infinite regress of bringings-about, which is impossible. The conclusion is that power over an action is impossible, implying determinism (jabr).

Checkpoint
According to the second refutation, why does separating bringing about (īqāʿ) and occurrence (wuqūʿ) lead to an impossibility?
A) Because bringing about and occurrence are the same thing.
B) Because it creates an infinite regress of bringings-about, each needing prior power.
C) Because the agent cannot have power in the first moment.
D) Because the objection assumes motivations are balanced.

Key Takeaway
The objection of postponed preponderance is refuted by showing it either repeats the original dilemma or leads to an infinite regress of power acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, where do you assume that Allah's power can be 'postponed' — that you can delay seeking His help or put off obedience without immediate consequences? How does the lesson's rejection of temporal separation between bringing about and occurrence confront that assumption? What will you do today to close that gap?
?
Read the second argument again: every bringing about requires a prior bringing about, leading to an infinite regress. Now examine your own heart: where have you created a gap between your intention to change and your actual action? Are you waiting for a 'second moment' that never comes? Confess that now and commit to immediate action, trusting in Allah's power that is simultaneous with your act.
?
The objector thought postponement was possible, but Sanusi shows it leads to absurdity. Have you ever postponed a repentant action, thinking you could 'bring it about' later? How does this lesson prove that your own power cannot be separated from the moment? What will you bring about right now, not in a future moment?

Causality and Created Things
advanced
12 min
The Impossibility of Action in a Future Time
Refutation of performing an action in a future moment while present in the present.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is rationally impossible for a capable agent to perform an action in a future moment while being present only in the present moment, and how the distinction between 'performance' and 'occurrence' leads to an infinite regress.

Core Idea
You cannot act in a future time because it leads to an infinite chain of performances.

This lesson unpacks a subtle argument from classical Islamic theology about the nature of action and time. The author, likely drawing on Ash'ari or Maturidi reasoning, targets the idea that an agent can 'perform' an action at a future moment while only existing in the present. By analyzing the relationship between the performance and the occurrence of an act, he demonstrates a logical contradiction. Prepare to think carefully about causality, power, and temporal succession.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the key terms: إيقاع (performance) and وقوع (occurrence). Pay attention to how the argument builds.
The passage is dense, so take your time.
2
Identify the two main parts of the argument
The argument first states a direct impossibility: performing in the second moment while in the first is impossible because the second moment is absent. Then it considers an alternative scenario where performance occurs in the first moment and occurrence in the second.
3
Trace the infinite regress
Follow the reasoning: If performance is distinct from occurrence and caused by the agent's power, then that performance itself must be caused by a prior performance, leading to an infinite chain. Understand why this is deemed impossible.
This is the core of the proof.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider how this argument supports the view that a capable agent's power is directly connected to the act in the present moment, and that temporal gaps cannot exist between power and its effect.
This connects to broader discussions of divine and human agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الزمان الثاني الذي هو ممتنع الحضور في الزمان الأول والموقوف على الممتنع ممتنع فوجب أن يكون إيقاع الفعل في الزمان الثاني عند حضور الزمان الأول ممتنعاً والممتنع لا قدرة عليه والثاني إن على هذا التقدير يكون إيقاع الفعل حاصلا في الزمان الأول ويكون وقوع الفعل حاصلا في الزمان الثاني فهذا يوجب أن يكون إيقاع الفعل مغايراً لوقوعه فنقول ذلك المغاير المسمى بالإيقاع إن لم يكن واقعاً بقدرة هذا الفاعل وجب أن يكون الفعل الواقع بسبب هذا الإيقاع غير واقع بهذا القادر وإن كان واقعاً بقدرة هذا الفاعل وجب أن تكون قدرته متقدمة عليه ثم الكلام فيه كما في الأول وهذا يوجب أن يكون كل إيقاع مسبوقاً بإيقاع آخر إلى غير النهاية بحسب الزمان وذلك يوجب أن تكون قدرة القادر متقدمة على حصول الأثر بأزمنة لا نهاية لها وكل ذلك محال
The second moment, which is impossible to be present in the first moment, and that which depends on the impossible is itself impossible, so it must be that performing the action in the second moment at the presence of the first moment is impossible. And the impossible cannot be within power. The second [point]: On this assumption, the performing of the action would occur in the first moment, and the occurrence of the action would happen in the second moment. This necessitates that the performing of the action is different from its occurrence. We say: That different thing called 'performance' – if it is not actualized by the power of this agent, then the action that occurs due to this performance would not be actualized by this capable being. And if it is actualized by the power of this agent, then his power must be prior to it. Then the discourse concerning it is as in the first [case]. This necessitates that every performance is preceded by another performance, ad infinitum with respect to time. And that necessitates that the power of the capable being precedes the attainment of the effect by infinite times. And all of that is impossible.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage presents an argument against the possibility of a human agent having genuine power to initiate an action at a future moment while being present in the present moment. The author first states that the second moment cannot be present in the first moment, so performing an action in the second moment while in the first is impossible. Then he considers the alternative: if we suppose that the performance occurs in the first moment and the action occurs in the second, then performance and occurrence are distinct. If this performance is not caused by the agent's power, then the action is not from him; if it is caused by his power, then his power must precede the performance, leading to an infinite regress of performances. Such infinite regress is impossible. Therefore, the initial assumption that the agent can perform an action in a future moment is invalid.

Key Terms
إيقاع (performance)
وقوع (occurrence)
ممتنع (impossible)
قدرة (power)
قادر (capable agent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies that actions can occur at different times than the agent's present.
Correction: The argument does not deny temporal succession; it denies that a 'performance' distinct from the action can logically bridge the temporal gap without causing an infinite regress.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what is impossible?
A) An action occurring in the future.
B) Performing an action in the second moment while present in the first.
C) An agent having power over an action.
D) The occurrence of an action in the present.

Key Takeaway
Acting in a future moment is impossible because it requires a separate 'performance' that either alienates the action from the agent or leads to an infinite regress of performances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you convinced yourself that you will 'perform' an action later, as if the future moment is already under your control? How does this proof expose the deception in that reasoning?
?
If your power (qudra) cannot reach a future moment except through an infinite chain, then every delay in obedience is actually an admission of powerlessness. What action have you been postponing that you must start now, not because you can do it later, but because you can only do it now?
?
The proof shows that attributing an action to yourself requires immediate causal connection. Is there a sin you committed in the past that you think you can 'undo' later? How does this passage force you to confront the impossibility of changing the past and the urgency of repentance now?

The Fourth Proof: Impossibility During the Action
Proof that capacity for both is impossible during the action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is impossible for a human to have the capacity to both act and refrain at the very moment of performing an action.

Core Idea
Capacity for both during the act requires opposites to coexist, which is impossible.

This lesson begins the fourth proof by presenting the overall division of times and focusing on the moment of action. The author argues that having the capacity to refrain while acting would require the impossible conjunction of action and refraining. We will examine why this is a logical impossibility and what it implies about human power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the opening division of times.
2
Identify the three times
The passage starts by stating that capacity would exist either during, before, or after the action. Note this tripartite division.
This structure is key to the entire proof.
3
Focus on the argument for 'during'
The author argues that capacity during the action is impossible. Identify the reasoning: it would require the conjunction of opposites.
4
Understand the conjunction of opposites
Reflect on why action and refraining are opposites and why their simultaneous existence is logically impossible.
This is a foundational concept in rational theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة المكنة من الفعل والترك لو حصلت لحصلت إما حال حصول الفعل أو قبل حصوله أو بعد حصوله والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة فالقول بحصول هذه المكنة باطل إنما قلنا إنه يمتنع حصول القدرة على الفعل حال حصوله لأن حصول الترك حال حصول الفعل محال لأنه يوجب الجمع بين النقيضين وذلك محال والمحال لا قدرة عليه فيثبت أن حال حصول الفعل يمتنع كونه قادراً على الفعل والترك وإنما قلنا إنه يمتنع حصول القدرة على الفعل قبل حصوله لأنه إما أن يكون المراد أنه قبل حصول الفعل يكون موصوفاً بأنه عند مجيء الزمان الثاني فإنه يصير موصوفاً بأنه يقدر على الفعل أو يكون المراد أنه عند حصول الوقت الأول يمكنه إيقاع الفعل في الزمان الثاني منه والأول محال لأن عند حضور ذلك الوقت إن كان الفعل حاضراً فلا قدرة فيه على الترك وإن كان الترك حاضراً فلا قدرة فيه على الفعل لأن ذلك يوجب الجمع بين النقيضين والثاني أيضاً باطل لأن عند حضور الزمان الأول يمتنع حضور الزمان الثاني وإيقاع الفعل في الزمان الثاني موقوف على حضور الزمان الثاني والموقوف على المحال محال والمحال لا قدرة عليه وإنما قلنا إنه يمتنع حصول القدرة على الفعل بعد حصول الفعل لأن ذلك معلوم بالبديهة فيثبت أن القدرة على الفعل بمعنى حصول المكنة من الفعل والترك ممتنعة الوجود قبل الفعل ومعه وبعده فكان القول بثبوت هذه القدرة محالا
The fourth proof: The capacity [to perform] the action and refraining, if it existed, it would exist either at the moment of the action's occurrence, or before its occurrence, or after its occurrence. And all three divisions are invalid, so the statement that this capacity exists is invalid. We say: It is impossible for power over the action to exist at the moment of its occurrence because refraining at the moment of action is impossible, as it would necessitate the conjunction of two opposites, and that is impossible, and the impossible cannot be within power. So it is established that at the moment of the action, it is impossible for [the agent] to be capable of both action and refraining. And we say: It is impossible for power over the action to exist before its occurrence because either the intended meaning is that before the action he is described such that when the second moment arrives he becomes described as capable of the action, or the intended meaning is that at the first time he can perform the action in the second moment. The first is impossible because at the presence of that time, if the action is present, then there is no power to refrain, and if refraining is present, then there is no power to act, because that would necessitate the conjunction of two opposites. The second is also invalid because at the presence of the first moment, the presence of the second moment is impossible, and performing the action in the second moment depends on the presence of the second moment, and that which depends on the impossible is itself impossible, and the impossible cannot be within power. And we say: It is impossible for power over the action to exist after the action because that is known intuitively. So it is established that power over the action, meaning the capacity for both action and refraining, its existence before, during, and after the action is impossible. Thus, the assertion of the existence of this capacity is impossible.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage presents the Fourth Proof, which argues that the capacity (al-makna) to both perform and refrain from an action is impossible. The author divides the possible times such capacity could exist — during, before, or after the action — and demonstrates each is impossible. During the action, having the capacity to refrain would require the simultaneous existence of action and refraining, a contradiction. Before the action, two interpretations are considered: either the agent is described as having the capacity at a future moment, which fails because at that future moment the action or refraining would be present, again a contradiction; or the agent is said to be able to perform in the second moment from the first, which fails because the second moment is not present. After the action, the impossibility is obvious. Therefore, such capacity cannot exist at any time, refuting those who claim it.

Key Terms
مكنة (capacity)
قدرة (power)
جمع النقيضين (conjunction of opposites)
ممتنع (impossible)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People might think the argument denies any human power during action.
Correction: It only denies the capacity to both act and refrain at the same instant, not power to act itself.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say capacity during action is impossible?
A) Because time is too short
B) Because it would require the conjunction of opposites
C) Because human power is limited
D) Because action and refraining are the same

Key Takeaway
At the moment of action, it is impossible to have the capacity to both perform and refrain from that action because that would require opposites to coexist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have committed a sin, have you ever comforted yourself with the thought 'I could have chosen otherwise'? This lesson argues that at the very moment of the act, you lacked the capacity to both act and refrain. If that is true, how does it change your understanding of your own responsibility? Spend a minute in silence, then sincerely ask Allah to forgive you for every moment you acted while claiming a false sense of independent choice.
?
Consider a recent decision you made—maybe something small like speaking harshly. At that moment, were you truly capable of both speaking and remaining silent with equal power? If not, where does that leave your pride in your own free will? Tonight, before sleeping, identify one such incident and write down: 'At that moment, I was not capable of the opposite.' Then thank Allah that He is the only One with absolute power, and beg Him to grant you true power to obey.
?
If you cannot have both action and refraining at the same moment, then your very existence of action or refraining is solely from Allah's creation. Does this make you feel like a puppet? Or does it deepen your reliance on Him? Commit today to say 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah' (There is no might nor power except with Allah) every time you catch yourself acting as if you are the independent source of your choices.

The Fourth Proof: Impossibility Before the Action
Proof that capacity for both is impossible before the action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two interpretations of capacity before action and why each is impossible.

Core Idea
Capacity before action fails under both interpretations due to temporal contradictions.

Having established impossibility during the action, the author now examines whether capacity could exist before the action. Two possible understandings are considered and both are shown to lead to contradiction or dependence on an impossibility. We will work through each interpretation carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation. Pay attention to the two 'either...or' structures.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The first interpretation: the agent is described as having capacity when the second moment arrives. The second: the agent can perform in the second moment from the first moment.
3
Understand why the first is invalid
At the second moment, either the action or refraining is present, so the agent would have power only over one, not both. This leads to contradiction.
4
Understand why the second is invalid
The second moment is not present at the first moment, so performing in the second depends on the impossible presence of that future moment. What depends on the impossible is itself impossible.

Source Text

The Fourth Proof: Impossibility After the Action and Conclusion
Proof that capacity for both is impossible after the action, and conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why capacity after action is intuitively impossible and how the three proofs together establish the impossibility of the claimed capacity.

Core Idea
Capacity after action is impossible by intuition, and the three cases together refute the claim of a capacity for both.

The final part of the fourth proof addresses capacity after the action, which is shown to be impossible through intuitive knowledge. The argument then concludes that since capacity cannot exist at any of the three possible times, the very concept of a capacity to both act and refrain is invalid. This lesson ties together the entire proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the short Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the use of 'badiha' (intuition).
2
Understand the intuitive impossibility
The author says it is known by intuition that capacity after action is impossible. Reflect on why this is self-evident.
What is past cannot be changed, so power over it is meaningless.
3
See the conclusion
The author summarizes that all three temporal states (before, during, after) are impossible, so the claim of such a capacity is impossible.
This completes the fourth proof.

Source Text

Causality and Created Things

The Fifth Argument: Perfect Equal Capacity Leads to Necessity or Impossibility
Proof that perfect equal capacity results in either impossibility or necessity of action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument from perfect equal capacity demonstrates that human power cannot be truly equal over opposites.

Core Idea
Perfect equal capacity always results in either impossibility or necessity, negating genuine power.

This lesson examines the fifth proof from the Al-Matalib al-Aliyah. The author presents a logical argument showing that if a person had perfectly equal capacity to act or refrain, the presence or absence of a determining motive would make the action either impossible or necessary. Since we have no power over the impossible or the necessary, such equal capacity cannot exist. This argument forces us to reconsider the nature of human power and its relationship to motives.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the logical flow of the argument. Pay attention to the key terms.
2
Identify the two cases
Notice that the argument splits into two possibilities: either a determining motive is present or absent. Each leads to a different outcome.
The determining motive is what tips the balance.
3
Analyze the outcomes
Both outcomes—impossibility and necessity—are states over which one has no power. Reflect on why this matters.
If the action is impossible or necessary, it cannot be an object of power.
4
Connect to the broader inquiry
The author mentions that this argument is derived from the first investigation into the nature of power. Consider how this fits into the larger discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة إن المكنة التامة المتساوية بالنسبة إلى طرفي الفعل والترك إذا حصلت فإن لم تحصل معها الداعية المرجحة امتنع صدور الفعل عنها وإن حصلت الداعية المرجحة فتلك القدرة مع تلك الداعية المرجحة الخالية عن المعارض تكون موجبة للفعل فعلى هذا الحاصل على أحد التقديرين هو الامتناع وعلى التقدير الثاني حصل الوجوب ولا قدرة البتة لا على الممتنع ولا على الواجب فوجب أن لا تحصل المكنة من الفعل البتة وهذه الوجوه الخمسة مستفادة من البحث الأول عن حال القدرة وعن كيفية تأثيرها في المقدور
The fifth proof: Perfect equal capacity with respect to the two aspects of action and refraining — if it exists, then if the determining motive does not accompany it, the action's occurrence from it is impossible; and if the determining motive accompanies it, then that power together with that determining motive free of any counterweight will be necessitating of the action. Thus, in one of the two cases the result is impossibility, and in the second case necessity has occurred. And there is no power at all over the impossible nor over the necessary. So it must be that the capacity for the action does not exist at all. And these five aspects are derived from the first investigation concerning the state of power and how it affects the object of power.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage presents the Fifth Proof. The author argues that if a human had a perfect and equal capacity for both action and refraining, then either a determining motive (al-da'iya al-murajjiha) is present or not. If there is no determining motive, the action cannot occur, making it impossible. If there is a determining motive without a counterweight, the power becomes necessitating (mujiba), making the action necessary. Both impossibility and necessity are not subject to power — one cannot have power over the impossible or the necessary. Therefore, such a capacity cannot exist. The author adds that these five arguments are derived from the first inquiry into the nature of power and its effect.

Key Terms
مكنة تامة
داعية مرجحة
موجبة للفعل
امتناع
وجوب
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans have full free will to choose regardless of motives.
Correction: The argument shows that if capacity were truly equal, external motives would determine the outcome, negating genuine choice. Human power is not independent of motives.

Checkpoint
What happens when a determining motive accompanies the perfect equal capacity?
A) The capacity becomes neutral.
B) The action becomes impossible.
C) The action becomes necessary.
D) The agent gains free will.

Key Takeaway
Perfect equal capacity cannot exist because it would lead to either impossibility or necessity, both of which are not subject to power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel equally able to do or not do an action, is that feeling genuine? Or are you merely unaware of the motives that already determine your decision? Can you recall a specific moment today when you faced a choice and believed you had perfect equal capacity? What does this argument reveal about that moment?
?
If perfect equal capacity leads to either impossibility or necessity, then your belief in your own free will is challenged. Which of your daily choices, if any, are truly free from determining motives? As you examine your heart, are you comfortable with the implication that your actions are necessitated? How does that change the way you seek Allah's help?
?
This proof claims that genuine power cannot exist over impossible or necessary actions. Consider a sin you commit repeatedly. Is it impossible for you to stop because no determining motive is strong enough? Or is it necessary because your motives compel you? What does that reveal about the state of your heart, and what step will you take today to seek a determining motive for obedience?

The Sixth Argument: Man's Ignorance of Details of Actions
Proof that man lacks knowledge of action details, so intention cannot produce the action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the lack of detailed knowledge of one's own actions demonstrates that the human cannot be the true cause of his actions.

Core Idea
If you don't know the details, you can't intend them, so you can't cause them.

This lesson presents the sixth proof from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah, which argues that human beings lack knowledge of the intricate details of their own actions, such as the exact number of atoms moved. Since intention requires knowledge, the absence of knowledge means the action cannot be truly caused by the human's own performance and bringing into existence. This argument challenges the notion of human causal agency and points to divine causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical structure: premise, example, reasoning, conclusion.
2
Identify the example and key concepts
The argument uses the example of moving a finger. Identify the key terms: individual atoms (جواهر فردة), spaces (أحياز), intention (قصد), knowledge (علم), performance (إيقاع), bringing into existence (إيجاد).
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow the argument from the premise (lack of knowledge of details) to the conclusion (impossibility of human causation). Note how each step builds on the previous one.
Write down the logical chain in your own words.
4
Consider the misconception
The argument does not deny that humans intend general actions, but that the specific details cannot be intended due to lack of knowledge. Reflect on this nuance.
This is a common misunderstanding that the commentary clarifies.
5
Reflect on the implication for human agency
Think about what this argument implies about human free will and causal responsibility. How does it point to Allah as the true creator of actions?
This connects to broader discussions in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة إن العبد غير عالم بتفاصيل أفعال نفسه لأنه إذا حرك إصبعه فهو لا يعرف أنه كم عدد من الجواهر الفردة حركها وفي كم عدد من الأحياز حرك ذلك الإصبع واختصاص العدد المعين بالوقوع دون الزائد والناقص لا بد وأن يكون بالقصد لكن القصد مشروط بالعلم فإذا لم يحصل العلم لم يحصل القصد أيضاً فامتنع كونه واقعاً بإيقاعه موجوداً بإيجاده
The sixth proof: The servant is not knowledgeable of the details of his own actions, because when he moves his finger, he does not know how many individual atoms he moved, nor in how many spaces he moved that finger. And the specification of the specific number occurring rather than the greater or lesser must be by intention, but intention is conditioned by knowledge. So if knowledge is not obtained, intention is also not obtained. Thus, it is impossible that [the action] occurs by his performance and exists by his bringing into existence.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage presents the Sixth Proof, arguing that humans do not have detailed knowledge of their actions. Using the example of moving a finger, the author points out that a person does not know the exact number of atoms (jawāhir farda) moved or the precise spaces traversed. Yet the specific occurrence of that particular number and position is determined. Such specification requires intention (qasd), but intention depends on knowledge. Since knowledge of these details is absent, intention is also absent. Therefore, the action cannot be truly caused by the human agent's own performance and bringing into existence.

جواهر فردة (individual atoms)
أحياز (spaces)
قصد (intention)
علم (knowledge)
إيقاع (performance)
إيجاد (bringing into existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies that humans intend any aspect of their actions.
Correction: The argument only denies that humans intend the specific details (like exact atoms moved), not that they have general intentions.

Checkpoint

A) Because the human intends the action but lacks power.
B) Because the human lacks knowledge of the details, so intention is absent.
C) Because the action is predetermined by God.
D) Because the human's finger moves on its own.

Key Takeaway
Because humans lack knowledge of the detailed specifications of their actions, they cannot truly cause those actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The proof shows you cannot intend what you do not know. Are there areas in your life where you act without understanding the details, yet you assume full responsibility? What would it look like to surrender that illusion of control to Allah?

The Seventh Proof: Conditional Premise for Refutation
Fragment of a proof that human power cannot be effective for creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Seventh Proof uses a conditional statement as a premise to argue that human power cannot create, and why this is a crucial method in kalam for establishing tawheed.

Core Idea
Kalam often disproves falsehood by assuming it and showing contradiction.

This fragment introduces the Seventh Proof with a conditional premise: if human power could create, what would follow? Even though the argument is incomplete here, we can see the method. Kalam theologians use hypothetical reasoning to refute views that compromise Allah's exclusive creative power. This lesson examines the conditional as a starting point for that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the conditional statement
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice it begins with 'if' (لو). This is a hypothetical assumption, not an assertion.
The full argument would show why this assumption is impossible.
2
Identify the intended purpose
Understand that this conditional is a premise for a refutation. The author intends to prove that human power cannot create by showing that the assumption leads to a contradiction.
This method is called 'proof by assumption' or 'impossibility argument'.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
Ask yourself: why is it important to prove that human power cannot create? This protects the uniqueness of Allah as the sole creator.
This is a central tenet of Islamic theology (tawheed ar-rububiyyah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة لو كانت قدرة العبد صالحة لإيجاد بعض الممكنات
The seventh proof: If the power of the servant were suitable for bringing into existence some contingent things...
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the Seventh Proof, which is incomplete in this window. The author states a conditional: 'if the power of the servant were suitable for bringing into existence some contingent things...' The argument presumably continues to show that this leads to a contradiction or impossibility, but the full reasoning is missing as the page ends. The fragment sets up a scenario to be refuted.

قدرة العبد (power of the servant)
صالحة لإيجاد (suitable for bringing into existence)
بعض الممكنات (some contingent things)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: This statement shows that some Muslim theologians believed that human power can create things.
Correction: It is a hypothetical premise for a refutation; the proof aims to show the impossibility of that premise.

B) If the servant's power were suitable for bringing into existence some contingent things.
C) If the servant's power were equal to God's power.
D) If the servant's power could create nothing.

Key Takeaway
The Seventh Proof begins by assuming the opposite of the truth and then demonstrating it leads to contradiction.

Proof Against Servant Creating Any Possible
Proof from shared possibility to impossibility of human creation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that if a human could create one possible thing, they could create all possible things, and why that is impossible, leading to the conclusion that no possible thing can be created by a human.

Core Idea
If you could create one possible, you could create all; you can't, so you create none.

This lesson presents a concise proof from the classical kalam tradition that denies the human servant any creative power. The argument turns on the nature of possibility (imkan) and its relationship to power. By following the logical steps, you will see why the theologian concludes that no possible thing can be created by a human.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then consult the translation. Notice the logical flow: a conditional statement, a reason, a negation, and a conclusion.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the conditional and its basis
The argument starts with a conditional: if the servant's power were valid for creating some possible, it would be valid for all. The basis is that possibility (imkan) is the criterion, and it's a single shared concept.
The term 'maqduriyya' refers to being within the scope of power.
3
See why the consequent is false
The argument states that the servant being able to create all possibles is false, because it would imply absurdities like creating himself and his own power.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since the conditional leads to a false consequent, the antecedent must be false. Therefore, the servant cannot create any possible thing at all.
This conclusion is absolute: not even one possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ لكانت صالحة لإيجاد كل الممكنات (۱) ] لأن المصحح للمقدورية هو الإمكان والإمكان مفهوم واحد مشترك فيه بين كل الممكنات ، لكن كون العبد قادراً على إيجاد كل الممكنات : باطل . وإلا لقدر على إيجاد نفسه ، وعلى إيجاد قدرته . فوجب أن لا يقدر على إيجاد شيء من الممكنات
[It would be valid for the creation of all possible things (1)] because the rectifier of being within power (maqduriyya) is possibility (imkan), and possibility is one concept shared among all possible things. But the servant being powerful over creating all possible things is false. Otherwise he would be able to create himself and to create his power. So it necessarily follows that he is not able to create any of the possible things.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (hujja) that the human servant cannot be a creator (mujid) of any possible thing. The argument begins by stating a conditional: if the servant's power were valid for creating some possible things, then it would be valid for creating all possible things. The reason given is that the factor that makes an action within the scope of power (maqduriyya) is possibility (imkan), and possibility is a single undifferentiated concept shared among all possible things. Therefore, if power applies to one possible, it should apply to all. However, it is false that the servant can create all possible things, because that would imply he could create himself and his own power—both impossibilities. Therefore, the original assumption must be false, and the servant cannot create any possible thing at all.

Key Terms
مقدورية
إمكان
إيجاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans have no power at all (qudra) over their actions.
Correction: This argument only denies the power to create (ijad) something from nothing, not the power to act (kasb) within God's creation.

Checkpoint
According to the first unnumbered argument, why would the servant be able to create all possible things if he could create any one of them?
A) Because all possible things are identical in essence.
B) Because the criterion for being within power (maqduriyya) is possibility, which is a single concept common to all possibles.
C) Because God grants him power over all possibles.
D) Because the servant's power is unlimited.

Key Takeaway
The human servant cannot create any possible thing because the ability to create any one possible would entail the ability to create all, which is absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself thinking that your success, intelligence, or even your next breath is something you have produced? This proof says you create nothing. How does that truth sit with your sense of self? What would it mean to truly internalize that every good in your life is a gift, not an achievement?
?
If your power does not create anything, then the very plan you made for your life, the will to follow it, and the steps you take are all sustained by Allah each moment. Are you willing to surrender your plans to Him, even the ones you hold most dear? What would that look like in practice today?
?
This argument shows that claiming any creative power for yourself leads to absurdities (like creating your own self). Is there any area of your life where you subtly act as if you are the source—whether in parenting, work, or spirituality? What would change if you replaced that attitude with complete reliance on Allah?

Eighth Proof: Servant's Object of Power and God's Power
Proof that the servant's maqdur is also God's, leading to impossibility of two independent causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eighth proof that demonstrates why the servant cannot be an independent creator alongside God, based on the similarity of objects of power and the impossibility of two independent causes producing the same effect.

Core Idea
Two independent causes cannot produce the same effect; divine power is uniquely capable.

This lesson presents one of the most sophisticated arguments against human creation, known as the eighth proof. The argument begins by establishing that any act falling under human power also falls under divine power, because the two objects of power are similar. From there, it proceeds to show that if both God and the servant were independent causes of the same act, logical absurdities follow. The only consistent conclusion is that the act is solely from God, negating the servant's role as a creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing deeply. Then read the English translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
Notice the logical connectors like 'because', 'so', 'but' that structure the proof.
2
Identify the core premise
The proof starts by stating that the servant's object of power (maqdur) is like God's object of power. This similarity is key. Ask yourself: why does the author assert this?
The concept of 'mithl' (similar) is important here.
3
Understand the expansion on similarity
The passage explains that two similar things are equal in the permissibility of contingent occurrences. Since God's essence necessitates power over His own maqdur, it must also necessitate power over the servant's maqdur because of the similarity.
This step establishes that God has power over the servant's maqdur.
4
Follow the argument against two independent causes
Once it is established that both God and the servant have power over the same maqdur, the proof entertains three possibilities: both cause it, one causes it, or only God causes it. It rules out both (repetition of existent) and one without a preferrer (arbitrary), leaving only God as the sole cause.
This is the heart of the proof; pay attention to the concepts of 'istisna' (independence) and 'murajjih' (preferrer).
5
Grasp the conclusion and implications
The proof concludes that the act occurs by God's power alone, preventing the servant from being a creator (mujid). This defends the Ash'ari position that human actions are acquired (kasb) rather than created.
The proof does not deny human agency but denies independent creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : إن مقدور العبد : مقدور الله تعالى [ فوجب أن يقدر (۲) ] لأن مقدور العبد مثل مقدور الله [ والمثلان يتساويان في جواز الجائزات ، فكان مقدور العبد ، يصح أن يكون مقدوراً الله (۳) ] تعالى ، وإذا ثبت القول بهذه الصحة - والموجب لقادرية الله تعالى هو ذاته المخصوصة ، ونسبة ذلك الإيجاب إلى الشيء ، كنسبته إلى مثله ، فلما أوجبت ذاته القادرية على أحد المثلين - وجب أن توجب (1) القادرية على المثل الثاني . فيثبت : أن مقدور العبد ، مقدور الله تعالى . وإذا ثبت هذا ، كان وقوعه بهما: محالا . لأن أحد السببين لما كان مستقلا بالاقتضاء ، فلو أثر الثاني فيه ، كان ذلك إيجاد للموجود ، وهو محال . فوجب أن يكون واقعاً بأحدهما (ه) لكن هذا محال . لأنه لما كان واحد منهما سبباً مستقلاً بالاقتضاء ، فوقوعه بأحدهما دون الثاني ، يقتضي رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن [ على الآخر (1) من غير مرجح . وهو محال . ولما بطل هذا ، كان وقوعه بقدرة الله تعالى أولى . وذلك يمنع من كون العبد موجدا(۷)
The eighth proof: The object of power (maqdur) of the servant is an object of power of God the Exalted, [so necessarily He has power over it (2)] because the object of power of the servant is like the object of power of God, [and two similar things are equal in the permissibility of contingent things, so the object of power of the servant is valid to be an object of power of God (3)] the Exalted. And when this validity is established — and that which necessitates the almightiness (qadiriyya) of God the Exalted is His specific essence, and the relation of that necessitation to a thing is like its relation to its like — so when His essence necessitated almightiness over one of the two similars, it must necessitate (1) almightiness over the second similar. Thus it is established that the object of power of the servant is an object of power of God the Exalted. And when this is established, its occurrence by both is impossible, because when one of the two causes is independent in efficacy, if the second were to effect it, that would be bringing about the existent, which is impossible. So it must occur by one of them (4). But this is impossible, because when each of them is an independent cause in efficacy, its occurrence by one without the other requires an inclination of one of the two sides of the possible [over the other (5)] without a preferrer (murajjih), which is impossible. And when this is invalid, its occurrence by God's power is more appropriate. And that prevents the servant from being a creator (mujid) (7).
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof against human creation. It begins by stating that the object of power (maqdur) of the servant is also an object of power of God, because the servant's maqdur is similar (mithl) to God's maqdur, and two similar things are equal in terms of the permissibility of contingent occurrences. Therefore, if it is valid for God to have power over His maqdur, it must also be valid for Him to have power over the servant's maqdur, since the ground of His power (His essence) relates to everything alike. Thus, both God and the servant would have power over the same object. However, two independent causes cannot both produce the same effect, because if one is sufficient, the other's involvement would be a repetition of existence, which is impossible. Nor can the effect occur by only one of them without a preferrer (murajjih) to choose between the two equals, which is also impossible. Therefore, the only valid conclusion is that the effect occurs by God's power alone, not the servant's, thus denying that the servant creates.

Key Terms
مقدور
قادرية
مثل
اقتضاء
مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies any reality to human actions or that humans have any role.
Correction: The argument does not claim that human actions have no reality; it argues against an independent human power of creation (ijad) concurrent with divine power. The Ash'ari position affirms human acquisition (kasb) while denying independent creation.

Checkpoint
What does the eighth proof conclude about the servant's ability to create?
A) The servant can create independently of God.
B) The servant and God both create the same act simultaneously.
C) The occurrence of any act must be by God's power alone, preventing the servant from being a creator.
D) The servant's power is equal to God's power.

Key Takeaway
The eighth proof shows that attributing creation to the servant leads to logical contradictions, so the act must be solely from God, thereby negating human creation in favor of acquisition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every action you take is only possible because of God's power, or do you still feel a sense of independent ability? How would your daily routine change if you consistently remembered that you are not the ultimate cause of your own actions?
?
When you succeed in something, do you ever consider that it was God's power that made it happen, or do you attribute it to your own skill? What would it mean for your pride to accept that you are not an independent creator?
?
If the servant has no independent power of creation, what does that mean for your moments of despair and your feelings of helplessness? Are you ready to attribute both your achievements and your failures entirely to God's power and wisdom?

Causality and Created Things

The Conflict of Intentions Proof
Reductio ad absurdum proof against independent human creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ninth proof from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah that shows the absurdity of independent human creation through a scenario of conflicting divine and human intentions.

Core Idea
Conflicting intentions between God and a servant would lead to logical impossibility.

This passage presents a reductio ad absurdum argument against the idea that a human servant could independently create. If the servant could create, then his intention could conflict with God's intention. The author explores the logical outcomes of such a conflict and shows that all possibilities lead to contradiction, thereby proving that independent creation is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical structure: a supposition (if servant could create), a scenario (conflicting intentions), and an analysis of possibilities.
The argument assumes a single body with two contradictory states: motion and stillness.
2
Identify the four possibilities
The passage lists three possibilities: both intentions realized, both prevented, or one realized without the other. Why are each of these impossible? Trace the reasoning.
The fourth possibility (both realized) is dismissed as impossible because a body cannot be both moving and still.
3
Understand the key concepts
Focus on the terms 'preventer' (man'e), 'effect' (ma'lul), 'power' (quwwa), 'strength' (quwwa/da'f), and 'preference without preferrer' (rujhan bila murajjih). These are crucial for the proof.
The argument relies on the indivisibility of the single act and equality of powers.
4
Follow the logical chain
Trace how the impossibility of both prevented leads to a causal loop, and how the impossibility of one occurring without the other leads to the absurdity of a preference without a preferrer.
This step solidifies the conclusion that the servant cannot be an independent creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : لو صح الإيجاد من العبد . فإذا قصد العبد تحريك جسم ، وقصد الله تعالى تسكينه ، فإما أن يحصل المرادان معاً وهو محال أو يمتنعا . وهو أيضاً : محال . لأن المانع لكل واحد منهما عن تحصيل مراده : هو وقوع مراد الثاني. والمعلول لا يحصل إلا عند حصول العلة ، فلو حصل الامتناعان معا ، لحصل التأثيران معا ، وذلك يوجب الجمع بين النقيضين ، أو يقع مراد أحدهما دون الثاني ، وذلك : محال . وذلك لأن الحركة الواحدة ، والسكون الواحد : لا يقبل التجزئة في العقل . وإذا كان ذلك كذلك ، كانت القدرة عليه (۱) قابلة للقوة والضعف . وإذا كان كذلك ، كانت قوة كل واحد منهما في هذا الأثر الواحد ، معادلة لقوة الآخر . فإذا امتنع الفعل على أحدهما ، أننا بينا حصول الاستواء في القوة : لزم الرجحان لا المرجح وتيسر للثاني ، مع [ وهو محال (٢) ] .
The ninth proof: If creation (ijad) by the servant were valid, then when the servant intends to move a body and God the Exalted intends to still it, either both intentions occur together — which is impossible — or both are prevented — which is also impossible, because the preventer for each of them from achieving his intention is the occurrence of the intention of the other, and the effect does not occur except upon the occurrence of its cause. So if both preventions occurred together, both effects would occur together, and that necessitates combining two contradictories. Or the intention of one of them occurs without the other, and that is impossible, because the single motion and the single stillness do not admit division in the intellect. And if that is so, power over it is capable of strength and weakness. And if that is so, the strength of each of them in this single effect is equal to the strength of the other. So if the act is impossible for one of them, while we have established equality in strength, then an inclination (rujhan) without a preferrer (murajjih) is necessary, and it is facilitated for the second, along with [that being impossible (2)].
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth proof, which uses a scenario of conflicting divine and human intentions. If the servant could independently create, then if he intends to move a body and God intends to still it, four possibilities arise: both intentions realized (impossible, as a body cannot be both moving and still), both prevented (also impossible, because each prevention depends on the other's intention being realized, leading to a causal loop), or one realized without the other. The last option is impossible because the act is a single indivisible unit; power over it is uniform and equal for both agents. If one agent is prevented while the other proceeds, that would require a preference (rujhan) without a preferrer, which is absurd. Thus, independent human creation leads to impossibility, proving that the servant cannot be a creator.

Key Terms
إيجاد
مراد
امتناع
قوة
رجحان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof denies that humans have any intentions or that their intentions matter.
Correction: The proof does not deny human intention; it denies that humans have the power to independently create effects that could override divine will. Human intentions are real but dependent on God's creative power.

Checkpoint

D) Because both intentions cannot be known.

Key Takeaway
If creation by the servant were valid, conflicting intentions would lead to absurdity; therefore, only God can create.

Al-Ash'ari on Miracles and the Philosophers' Inconsistency
States al-Ash'ari's position and the philosophers' contradictory acceptance of spontaneous generation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari's view on the possibility of miracles leads to a critique of the philosophers' denial, revealing their inconsistency in accepting spontaneous generation.

Core Idea
Philosophers deny miracles but accept spontaneous generation—a contradiction.

In this passage, the author presents al-Ash'ari's radical position that God can directly transform any substance, denying any fixed natural powers. He then turns to the philosophers, who uniformly reject miracles. However, he points out a crucial inconsistency: they themselves accept spontaneous generation, which is a breach of natural order. Let's examine this contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Understand al-Ash'ari's position
Read the first part where al-Ash'ari permits mountains turning to gold, rivers to blood, and humans arising without parents. Note that he denies all influences, natures, and powers.
This sets the stage for the critique.
2
Identify the philosophers' denial
The text states that philosophers are unanimous in denying miracles (khawariq al-'adat). Recognize that they reject any breach of natural law.
3
Spot the inconsistency
The author argues that philosophers are forced to acknowledge miracles in cases like spontaneous generation of humans from elements, which is itself a breach of natural order. Identify this contradiction.
This is the key argument of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثالثة: إن مذهبه أنه يجوز انقلاب الجبال ذهباً إبريزاً. ويجوز انقلاب مياه الأودية دماً، وغيره، ويجوز حدوث الإنسان من غير الأبوين. وبالجملة فينكر جميع التأثيرات والطبائع والقوى. وأما الفلاسفة فإنهم أطبقوا على إنكار خوارق العادات، إلا أنه يلزمهم القول بالاعتراف بها في المسائل: المسألة الأولى: إنهم جوزوا حدوث إنسان بالتولد لا بالتوالد. وقرروا ذلك بأن قالوا: البدن الإنساني إنما تولد من مقادير مخصوصة من العناصر.
The third issue: His doctrine is that it is permissible for mountains to turn into pure gold, and for river waters to turn into blood and other things, and for a human to arise without parents. In sum, he denies all influences, natures, and powers. As for the philosophers, they are unanimous in denying miracles (khawariq al-'adat), except that they are compelled to acknowledge them in the following issues: The first issue: They permitted the occurrence of a human by spontaneous generation (tawallud) not by reproduction (tawālud). And they established that by saying: The human body is only generated from specific quantities of the elements.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is the third issue concerning the possibility of miracles. It first presents al-Ash'ari's doctrine: it is permissible (ja'iz) for mountains to turn into pure gold, for rivers to turn into blood, and for a human to come into existence without parents. This means that for al-Ash'ari, there are no fixed natures or powers; God can change any substance into any other substance directly. The author then notes that the philosophers universally deny miracles (khawariq al-'adat). However, the author points out an inconsistency: the philosophers are forced to acknowledge some cases that are similar to miracles. Specifically, they accept spontaneous generation (tawallud) – the idea that a human can arise from non-living matter without reproduction (tawālud). They argue that the human body is generated from specific quantities of the elements. By accepting this, they admit a kind of event that breaks the usual pattern of nature, which contradicts their denial of miracles. The passage ends abruptly as the OCR cuts off; the argument likely continues with further discussion.

التولد (spontaneous generation)
التوالد (reproduction)
الطبائع (natures)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Philosophers are fully consistent in denying all miracles.
Correction: The author shows they accept spontaneous generation, which is also a breach of natural order, thus they are inconsistent.

The philosophers' acceptance of spontaneous generation undermines their denial of miracles, showing they are not fully consistent.

Philosophers' Proof of Miracles via Spontaneous Generation
Philosophers prove spontaneous human generation possible, forcing admission of miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers' own causal framework leads to the necessity of miracles (khawāriq al-‘ādāt) through the possibility of spontaneous human generation.

Core Idea
If spontaneous human generation is possible, miracles are inevitable.

In this lesson, we explore the first philosophical issue that turns the tables on those who deny miracles. The philosophers argue that a human could arise spontaneously from elemental mixing. This seemingly speculative claim carries a powerful conclusion: if such an event is possible, then the breaking of customary laws must be admitted, even by the philosophers themselves. Read on to see how they are caught in their own logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text from 'المسألة الأولى' to 'لازماً'. Notice the logical sequence: premise, mixing, temperament, soul, then the conditional chain.
The passage is one continuous argument.
2
Understand the argument structure
Identify the premises: (1) human body arises from specific quantities of elements, (2) mixing leads to balanced temperament, (3) from proper temperament a soul necessarily arises. Then the key: each step is possible, so the whole chain is possible.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion is that if spontaneous generation is possible, then khawāriq al-‘ādāt (miracles breaking customary laws) are necessary according to their own view. This refutes their denial.
The word 'لازماً' is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الأولى : إنهم جوزوا حدوث إنسان بالتولد لا بالتوالد . وقرروا ذلك بأن قالوا : البدن الإنساني إنما تولد من مقادير مخصوصة من العناصر الأربعة . فتلك المقادير اختلطت وامتزجت في مدة معلومة ، فحصل بسبب ذلك الامتزاج ، كيفية مزاجية معتدلة . وإذا تم حدوث البدن بهذا الطريق ، وجب حدوث النفس المتعلقة بتدبيره وحينئذ يتم تكون الإنسان . قالوا : إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إنه لا يمتنع حصول أجزاء مخصوصة من العناصر الأربعة على تلك المقادير المعلومة ، ولا يمتنع اختلاطها. وعند اختلاطها لا بد وأن يتكون ذلك المزاج ، وعند تكونه لا بد وأن تحدث تلك النفس . والموقوف على الممكن : ممكن فكان حدوث الانسان المعين على سبيل التولد : ممكناً ، وإذا كان ممكناً ، كان انخراق العادات على قولهم لازماً .
The first issue: They permitted the occurrence of a human being through spontaneous generation (tawallud) not through reproduction (tawālud). They established that by saying: The human body is generated from specific quantities of the four elements. Those quantities mixed and blended in a known period, and due to that blending, a balanced temperamental quality (kayfiyya mizājiyya) results. When the body's occurrence is completed in this way, the occurrence of the soul (nafs) that manages it becomes necessary, and then the formation of the human is complete. They said: If this is established, then we say: It is not impossible for specific parts of the four elements to occur in those known quantities, and their mixing is not impossible. And when they mix, that temperament must form, and when it forms, that soul must occur. And what depends on what is possible is itself possible. So the occurrence of a particular human by way of spontaneous generation is possible. And if it is possible, then the breaking of customary laws (khawāriq al-‘ādāt) is necessary according to their own view.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage presents the first philosophical issue that demonstrates the inevitability of miracles within the philosophers' own framework. The philosophers argue that spontaneous generation of a human being is logically possible: if the right quantities of the four elements mix in a specific period, a balanced temperament forms, and then a soul must arise to govern that body. Since the premises are possible, the conclusion—the existence of a human without parents—is possible. Therefore, they must admit that such an extraordinary event (khāriq al-‘āda) is possible, which contradicts their usual denial of miracles.

Key Terms
تولد
توالد
عناصر أربعة
مزاج
نفس
انخراق العادات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Philosophers completely deny miracles.
Correction: Philosophers' own causal framework forces them to admit the possibility of miracles, as shown by the spontaneous generation argument.

Checkpoint

A) That miracles are impossible
B) That the breaking of customary laws (khawāriq al-‘ādāt) is necessary
C) That the soul does not exist
D) That the four elements are eternal

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' argument for spontaneous human generation logically entails the possibility of miracles (khawāriq al-‘ādāt), undermining their own denial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
