Philosophers' Argument for Possibility of All Miracles
Philosophers argue that common matter and unknown celestial forms allow any miracle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how philosophers argue that the common matter and unknown celestial forms make every type of miracle theoretically possible.

Core Idea
Celestial forms are not fixed, so any extraordinary event is possible.

In this short passage, the philosophers present a key argument to ground the possibility of miracles. They point to a shared underlying matter in the sublunar world and the influence of celestial forms that are not fixed or fully known. This leads them to conclude that no miracle is inherently impossible. Let's examine their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal translation. Notice the concise structure of the argument.
The passage begins with 'المسألة الثانية' indicating this is the second issue in a series.
2
Identify the key components
Break down the argument into its premises: (1) There is a common matter shared by all things in the world of generation and corruption. (2) The specific form of a body comes from a celestial form. (3) Celestial forms are unfixed and unknown. (4) Therefore, any miraculous form is possible.
Focus on the logical flow from common matter to the conclusion.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why the unfixed nature of celestial forms is crucial. If they were fixed, only certain forms could appear. But since they are not, any extraordinary occurrence is admissible.
This directly challenges the idea that some miracles are impossible by nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية (١) : إن هيولى عالم الكون والفساد : هيولى مشتركة بين الكل . وإنما اختص هيولي الجسم المعين بالصورة [ المعينة لأن شكلاً فلكياً ، اقتضى كون تلك المادة مستعدة لقبول تلك الصورة (۳) ] الخاصة [ للتشكلات النامية (۳) ] والأشكال الفلكية غير مضبوطة ، وغير معلومة . وبهذا التقدير فإنه لا نوع من أنواع الخوارق إلا وهو ممكن محتمل .
The second issue: The matter (hayūlā) of the world of generation and corruption is common matter shared by everything. The matter of a particular body is distinguished by a specific form only because a celestial form necessitated that matter to be prepared to accept that particular form. And celestial forms are not fixed and are unknown. On this estimation, there is no type of miraculous occurrence (khawāriq) but that it is possible and admissible.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage presents the second philosophical argument for the possibility of miracles. The philosophers state that the underlying matter (hayūlā) of the sublunar world is common to all bodies. What makes a particular body have a specific form is the influence of a celestial form, which is not fixed (ghayr maḍbūṭa) and not known (ghayr ma‘lūma). Since these celestial forms are variable and unknown, any extraordinary form—any miracle—is possible and admissible. Thus, the philosophers must admit the theoretical possibility of all miracles.

Key Terms
هيولى
كون وفساد
شكل فلكي
خوارق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the philosophers deny any commonality in matter and believe each body has an entirely different substance, making miracles impossible.
Correction: The philosophers affirm a common substratum (hayūlā) shared by all bodies. The differentiation comes from celestial forms, which are not fixed, so any form—including miraculous ones—can be realized.

Checkpoint
Why do the philosophers argue that any miracle is possible?
A) Because matter is eternal
B) Because celestial forms are fixed
C) Because celestial forms are not fixed and are unknown
D) Because matter does not change

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that because celestial forms are unfixed and unknown, all miracles are theoretically possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If all miracles are possible, why do you sometimes feel that God is not able to answer your deepest prayers? Is it because you doubt His power or because you doubt your own worthiness? Commit to asking God for one thing you have given up on as impossible.

Two Examples of the Absurdity of Miracles
Two concrete examples showing the absurdity of miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses concrete examples to demonstrate that believing in miracles contradicts rational judgment.

Core Idea
If miracles are possible, then every rational mind is unreliable.

The author has already presented a logical proof against miracles. Now he drives the point home with two vivid examples that appeal to our intuitive sense of absurdity. These examples show that anyone who accepts miraculous transformations would be deemed insane by universal reason. Let's examine them closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the first example
Read the first example about water turning into blood and mountains into gold. Notice the immediate judgment of insanity.
The text says every rational person would judge such a person as insane.
2
Read the second example
Read the second example about a donkey becoming a wise teacher and insects becoming sages. Consider the absurdity of the scenario.
This example is more elaborate but cut off; the point is clear.
3
Reflect on the demonstration
Ask yourself why these examples are effective. They rely on universal human reason to reject impossible transformations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المثال الأول : لو أن إنساناً جوز أن تنقلب مياه البحر والأودية دماً عبيطاً وأن تنقلب الجبال ذهباً إبريزاً ، لقضى كل عاقل بالجنون عليه .
المثال الثاني : لو أن إنساناً جوز أن ينقلب الحمار الذي في بيته إنساناً حكيماً محيطاً بدقائق المنطق والهندسة ، مدرساً فيها ، وأن تنقلب ما في الدار من الخنافس والديدان : أناساً ، حكماء فضلاء ، وجوز إنه إذا رجع إلى بيته ، وجد حماره قائماً مقام بطليموس في تدريس كتاب المجسطي ووجد الخنافس
The first example: If a person allowed that the waters of the sea and the rivers could turn into fresh blood, and that the mountains could turn into pure gold, every rational person would judge that he is insane.
The second example: If a person allowed that the donkey in his house could turn into a wise human being, encompassing the subtleties of logic and geometry, teaching them, and that the beetles and worms in the house could turn into virtuous wise people, and allowed that when he returns to his house, he would find his donkey standing in the place of Ptolemy teaching the book of Al-Majisti, and he would find the beetles...
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage provides two concrete examples to demonstrate the unreasonableness of believing in miracles, following the earlier logical proof. The first example imagines someone allowing that sea and river waters could turn into fresh blood, or mountains into pure gold. Any rational person (kull ‘aqil) would immediately judge such a person to be insane. This shows that such miraculous transformations are considered impossible by universal reason. The second example is more elaborate: it imagines a person allowing that his donkey turns into a wise human being proficient in logic and geometry, and that insects in his house turn into virtuous sages. When he returns home, he finds his donkey acting as Ptolemy teaching the Al-Majisti (the Almagest). The example is cut off, but the point is clear: such a scenario is so absurd that no rational person would accept it as possible. These examples are meant to illustrate that miracles contradict our intuitive knowledge about the world and the natural order, reinforcing the earlier argument that miracles are impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These examples are arguments that God cannot perform miracles.
Correction: The examples are not about God's power but about the natural order that our minds find necessary. They show that believing in such transformations contradicts rational judgment.

Three Examples of Impossible Habit-Breaking
Three examples illustrating absurdity of arbitrary habit-breaking.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why deeming arbitrary violations of natural laws is considered rationally impossible, as demonstrated through three vivid examples.

Core Idea
Axiomatic reason demands continuity; arbitrary miracles are absurd.

The author presents three extreme scenarios to drive home a fundamental point: rational minds inherently expect natural laws to be consistent. Before we examine his conclusion, let's walk through each example and feel the force of their absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the three examples
Read each example carefully, imagining the scenario. Notice how each one becomes progressively more absurd.
The examples are vivid and designed to evoke immediate rational rejection.
2
Identify the common pattern
What do all three examples have in common? Each involves a massive, unmotivated change in the natural order without any apparent cause or purpose.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that axiomatic minds (badā'iʿ al-ʿuqūl) judge that states must continue on their usual paths. Deeming otherwise invalidates axiomatic sciences.
This is not a denial of all miracles, but of arbitrary violations.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this argument is important for theology. It sets a boundary: miracles are possible only through divine wisdom, not random absurdity.
This connects to the wider discussion of causality and divine action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المثال الأول: لو أن إنساناً جوز أن تنقلب مياه البحر والأودية دماً عبيطاً وأن تنقلب الجبال ذهباً إبريزاً، لقضى كل عاقل بالجنون عليه. المثال الثاني: لو أن إنساناً جوز أن ينقلب الحمار الذي في بيته إنساناً حكيماً محيطاً بدقائق المنطق والهندسة، مدرساً فيها، وأن تنقلب ما في الدار من الخنافس والديدان: أناساً، حكماء فضلاء، وجوز إنه إذا رجع إلى بيته، وجد حماره قائماً مقام بطليموس في تدريس كتاب المجسطي ووجد الخنافس والديدان: علماء فضلاء، يبحثون مع ذلك الحمار في دقائق الهندسة والمنطق والإلهيات، لقضى كل عاقل عليه بأعظم أنواع الجنون. المثال الثالث: لو أن إنساناً شاهد مغارة خالية عن جميع أنواع العمارات، ثم جوز أن يحصل فيها قصور عالية، وأبنية رفيعة، وأنهار جارية، من غير أن يتكفل بتلك العمارات أحد من البشر، ومن غير إحضار الخشب واللبن والمسامير، لقضى كل عاقل عليه بالجنون. فيثبت: أن بدائه العقول قاضية بوجوب استمرار هذه الأحوال على مناهجها الأصلية، ومجاريها المألوفة المعتادة، وثبت أن تجويز انقلابها عن مجاريها يقدح في العلوم البديهية، فوجب أن يكون القول به باطلا.
The first example: If a person considers it possible that the waters of the sea and valleys turn into fresh blood and that mountains turn into pure gold, every rational person would judge him to be insane. The second example: If a person considers it possible that the donkey in his house turns into a wise human being, encompassing the minutiae of logic and geometry, teaching them, and that the beetles and worms in the house turn into people, wise and virtuous, and he considers it possible that when he returns to his house, he finds his donkey standing in the place of Ptolemy teaching the Almagest, and finds the beetles and worms as virtuous scholars, discussing with that donkey the intricacies of geometry, logic, and theology, every rational person would judge him to be among the greatest types of insanity. The third example: If a person sees a cave empty of all types of buildings, then considers it possible that there occur in it high palaces, lofty structures, and flowing rivers, without any human undertaking those constructions, and without bringing wood, bricks, and nails, every rational person would judge him insane. So it is established: that the axiomatic minds judge the necessity of these states continuing on their original paths and their usual customary courses, and it is established that deeming their alteration from their courses invalidates the axiomatic sciences, so it must be that the statement of it is false.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage provides three examples to illustrate the absurdity of deeming possible violations of natural laws (khawariq al-'adat). The first example: someone considers it possible for sea water to become blood and mountains to become gold—any rational person would call him insane. The second example: someone considers it possible for a donkey to become a wise teacher and insects to become scholars discussing advanced subjects with the donkey—again, insanity. The third example: someone sees an empty cave and considers it possible for palaces and rivers to appear without builders or materials—also insanity. The conclusion: axiomatic minds judge that states must continue on their usual courses; deeming otherwise invalidates axiomatic science and is false.

Key Terms
بدائه العقول
انخراق العادات
المجاري المألوفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage denies all miracles, including prophetic miracles.
Correction: The passage only denies arbitrary, purposeless violations of natural laws. Prophetic miracles are purposeful and are not considered absurd because they serve a divine wisdom.

Checkpoint
What do the three examples collectively aim to show?
A) That miracles are impossible
B) That deeming any change in natural laws is absurd and invalidates axiomatic knowledge
C) That only prophets can perform miracles
D) That rational people believe in miracles

Key Takeaway
Axiomatic reason demands that natural states continue on their usual course; deeming arbitrary violations is rationally impossible and invalidates basic knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The author says deeming arbitrary violation invalidates axiomatic sciences. Are there any beliefs you hold—perhaps out of emotion or tradition—that contradict basic reason? Are you willing to examine them honestly?
?

Second Argument: Impossibility of Breaking Habits through Three Possibilities
Argument classifying three possibilities for physical attribute particularization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author argues that breaking habits is impossible by showing that any explanation for the particularization of physical attributes leads to contradiction or absurdity.

Core Idea
Three explanations for physical attributes all collapse — proving habits cannot break.

This lesson examines a second argument that habit-breaking is impossible. The author takes a case: each body has a specific attribute (e.g., fire burns). He then considers three ways this could be explained: by necessity, by preponderance, or by the free agent's choice without a reason. Each leads to a fatal problem. Follow the three branches to see the logical trap.

Guided Path
1
Understand the starting point
The argument begins with a concrete observation: every body has a specific attribute. For example, fire burns and water cools. The question is why this particularization holds.
2
First branch: necessity
If the particularization is necessary, then the attribute cannot change by definition. This makes habit-breaking impossible because the attribute would have to alter, contradicting its necessity.
Necessity implies immutability.
3
Second branch: preponderance
If it is merely preponderant (awla), then either the preponderance is essential (self-necessary) or derived from another attribute. Essential preponderance cannot cease; derived leads to infinite regress, implying eternity of the world, which invalidates prophethood.
Infinite regress is absurd in this context.
4
Third branch: free agent without cause
If the particularization is due to a free agent (Allah) without any preponderating factor, then miracles could also be created arbitrarily, undermining their function as proofs of truthfulness.
Arbitrary miracles lose evidential value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في بيان أن ذلك محال: أن نقول: اختصاص كل جسم بصفته المعينة، إما أن يكون لأجل أن اختصاصه بتلك الصفة واجب، أو إن لم يكن واجباً، لكنه أولى من غيره، أو لم يحصل هذا الوجوب، ولا هذه الأولوية. فإن حصل مع الوجوب، كان محالاً، وإن حصل مع الأولوية فتلك الأولوية إن كانت لنفس الذات، امتنع زوالها، وإن كان حصولها لصفة أخرى، كان الكلام في الثاني كما في الأول، فيلزم أن يكون حصول أولوية كل صفة، لأجل تقدم صفة أخرى لا إلى أول. وذلك يوجب القول [بقدم العالم، وهذا يبطل القول] بالنبوة. وأما القسم الثالث: وهو أن يقال: اختصاص كل جسم بصفته المعينة، لأجل أن الفاعل المختار، خصصه بتلك الصفة من غير مرجح اصلا. فنقول: إذا جوزتم هذا، فجوزوا أيضاً أن يخلق تلك المعجزة لا لمرجح أصلاً، وذلك يقدح في قولنا إنه إنما خلق المعجز لأجل التصديق. وإذا ثبت هذا فحينئذ يخرج المعجز من أن يكون دليلاً على الصدق. فالحاصل: أن اختصاص كل جسم بصفته المعينة. إن كان على سبيل الوجوب
The second aspect in explaining that that is impossible: we say: The particularization of every body with its specific attribute is either because its particularization with that attribute is necessary, or if not necessary, then it is more preponderant than others, or this necessity nor this preponderance occurs. If it occurs with necessity, it is impossible; and if it occurs with preponderance, then if that preponderance is for the essence itself, its removal is prevented; and if its occurrence is for another attribute, then the discussion about the second is like the discussion about the first, so it would necessitate that the occurrence of the preponderance of every attribute is due to the precedence of another attribute without end. That necessitates the statement [of the eternity of the world, and this invalidates the statement] of prophethood. As for the third division: that it is said: the particularization of every body with its specific attribute is due to the free agent specifying it with that attribute without any preponderating factor at all. We say: If you deem this possible, then also deem it possible that He creates that miracle without any preponderating factor at all, and that invalidates our statement that He only created the miracle for the sake of confirmation. And if this is established, then the miracle ceases to be a proof of truthfulness. The conclusion: that the particularization of every body with its specific attribute, if it is by way of necessity...
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument that breaking habits is impossible (muhal). It classifies the particularization of each body with its specific attribute into three possibilities: (1) It is necessary (wajib) – but if necessary, change is impossible. (2) It is preponderant (awla) – but if the preponderance is essential, it cannot cease; if it is due to another attribute, there is an infinite regress, which implies the eternity of the world, which contradicts prophethood. (3) It is by the free agent without any preponderating factor – but if that is possible, then miracles could also be created without a reason, which would undermine their function as proofs of prophethood. The text cuts off while discussing the third division, but the argument shows that any view leads to contradiction or absurdity.

Key Terms
اختصاص
الوجوب
الأولوية
الفاعل المختار
المرجح
المعجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies that God can perform miracles.
Correction: The argument does not deny miracles; it shows that arbitrary violations (without purpose) would destroy the evidential value of miracles, not that God is incapable of performing them.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, what is a consequence of saying that the particularization of bodies is by a preponderance based on another attribute (without end)?
A) It leads to the eternity of the world
B) It proves the existence of God
C) It supports the possibility of miracles
D) It is the correct view

Key Takeaway
The particularization of attributes cannot be arbitrary; every explanation leads to a logical impasse, showing that habit-breaking is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Examine your heart—do you truly believe they are, or do you have hidden doubts that this argument exposes? Commit today to strengthening your conviction by studying the evidences of prophethood more deeply.
?
The argument shows that God’s actions must have wise purposes. Think of a trial in your life that felt arbitrary or pointless. Does this lesson change how you view that trial? Can you now trust that behind every hardship is a specific wisdom from Allah, even if you do not see it? Resolve to remember this truth when difficulties come.
?
The third possibility warns that if attributes are assigned without reason, the entire purpose of creation collapses. Look at your own life: do you live as if every moment has a purpose from Allah, or do you sometimes act as if things happen by chance? What would it mean for your daily choices if you truly believed that nothing in your life is arbitrary?

The Cave Analogy: Rational Necessity of Natural Continuity
Third analogy proving innate reason demands natural continuity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how an analogy of an empty cave demonstrates that innate human reason demands the continuation of natural laws and that imagining their violation is irrational.

Core Idea
Innate reason compels us to expect nature to follow its familiar course.

The author presents a third analogy to show that human reason naturally expects consistency in the natural world. The example of an empty cave challenges us to consider whether we can rationally suppose that elaborate structures could arise without cause. This thought experiment reveals that anyone who denies natural continuity would be considered insane, proving that self-evident truths require nature's regularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the cave analogy carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the scenario: a man sees an empty cave and imagines palaces and rivers appearing without human effort.
This is a thought experiment.
2
Identify the rational judgment
The author says every rational person would judge the man insane. Ask yourself: Why is this judgment considered necessary and universal?
3
Connect to the conclusion
The passage concludes that innate rational principles require continuity of natural states. Understand how the analogy supports this: the irrationality of the imagined scenario proves that reason demands regularity.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why imagining deviation from natural courses would undermine self-evident sciences. The author argues that such imagination is inherently false.
This relates to the debate about miracles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المثال الثالث : لو أن إنساناً شاهد مغارة خالية عن جميع أنواع العمارات ، ثم جوز أن يحصل فيها قصور عالية ، وأبنية رفيعة ، وأنهار جارية ، من غير أن يتكفل بتلك العمارات أحد من البشر ، ومن غير إحضار الخشب واللبن والمسامير ، لقضى كل عاقل عليه بالجنون . فيثبت : أن بدائه العقول قاضية بوجوب استمرار هذه الأحوال على مناهجها الأصلية ، ومجاريها المألوفة المعتادة ، وثبت أن تجويز انقلابها عن مجاريها يقدح في العلوم البديهية ، فوجب أن يكون القول به باطلا
The third example: If a person witnessed a cave empty of all types of constructions, then imagined that there could occur in it high palaces, lofty buildings, and flowing rivers, without any human undertaking those constructions, and without bringing wood, bricks, and nails, every rational person would judge him to be insane. So it is established that innate rational judgments require the continuation of these states upon their original courses and their familiar, customary paths, and it is established that imagining their deviation from their courses undermines the self-evident sciences, so the statement of that must be false.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents a third example (analogy) to prove that rational necessity requires the continuation of natural laws, and that imagining their violation is contradictory to self-evident knowledge. The author describes a scenario: a completely empty cave, and someone supposes that without any human intervention or materials, magnificent palaces, lofty buildings, and flowing rivers could spontaneously appear. Every rational person would immediately call that person insane. From this, the author concludes that the innate principles of the intellect (badāʾiʿ al-ʿuqūl) judge that states continue according to their original, familiar, and customary patterns (manāhijuhā al-aṣliyya wa majārīhā al-maʾlūfa al-muʿtāda). Therefore, allowing the possibility of such deviation from their courses would damage the very foundation of self-evident sciences (al-ʿulūm al-badīhiyya). Hence, any claim that allows such violation is false.

Key Terms
بدائه العقول
استمرار الأحوال
مناهجها الأصلية
مجاريها المألوفة المعتادة
العلوم البديهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The cave analogy denies all miracles and supernatural events.
Correction: The analogy establishes that innate reason requires natural regularity. It does not deny that God can intervene as an exception; rather, it shows that any claim of deviation without special cause contradicts self-evident reason.

Checkpoint
According to the cave analogy, what would every rational person judge of someone who imagines palaces and rivers appearing in an empty cave without builders?
A) They would judge him to be a genius.
B) They would judge him to be insane.
C) They would judge him to be a philosopher.
D) They would judge him to be a skeptic.

Key Takeaway
Innate reason demands that natural states continue along their familiar courses, and any claim of deviation without special cause is intellectually indefensible.

Does this analogy strengthen your trust or create tension? Examine your heart: do you secretly doubt God's ability to act beyond the natural order?

The passage warns that imagining deviation from natural courses 'undermines the self-evident sciences.' What self-evident truths in your own faith or daily life risk being undermined when you allow your mind to doubt God's consistent patterns? Will you commit to grounding your expectations in the innate reason God gave you, even when life feels unpredictable?

The Trilemma of Natural Attributes and Miracles
Al-Razi's second argument proving violation of natural laws is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that any explanation of natural attributes either makes miracles impossible or undermines their evidential value.

Core Idea
If natural laws are fixed, miracles can't happen; if arbitrary, they can't prove truth.

This lesson examines Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's second argument against the possibility of miracles violating natural laws. He presents a trilemma: either the specific attributes of bodies are necessary, more suitable, or arbitrary. Each option, he argues, either prevents miracles or destroys their evidential power. By following his reasoning, you will see how he aims to show that the very concept of a miracle as evidence for prophethood is logically problematic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three alternatives
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how al-Razi divides the possibilities for why a body has a specific attribute.
The three alternatives are: necessity (wujūb), suitability (awlā), and arbitrary choice (lā murjīḥ).
2
Understand the consequences of necessity and suitability
Trace the argument: if necessity, change is impossible; if suitability, either it is essential (then permanent) or due to another attribute (leading to infinite regress and pre-eternity of the world, invalidating prophethood).
Both paths make violation of natural laws (miracles) impossible.
3
Grasp the problem with the arbitrary alternative
If the Free Agent specifies without reason, then miracles could be done without reason too, so they lose their role as evidence for truthfulness.
This undermines the evidential value of miracles, not their possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في بيان أن ذلك محال : أن نقول : اختصاص كل جسم بصفته المعينة ، إما أن يكون لأجل أن اختصاصه بتلك الصفة واجب ، أو إن لم يكن واجباً ، لكنه أولى من غيره ، أو لم يحصل هذا الوجوب ، ولا هذه الأولوية . فإن حصل مع الوجوب ، كان محالاً ، وإن حصل مع الأولوية فتلك الأولوية إن كانت لنفس الذات ، امتنع زوالها، وإن كان حصولها لصفة أخرى ، كان الكلام في الثاني كما في الأول ، فيلزم أن يكون حصول أولوية كل صفة ، لأجل تقدم صفة أخرى لا إلى أول . وذلك يوجب القول [ بقدم العالم ، وهذا يبطل القول ] بالنبوة . وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن يقال : اختصاص كل جسم بصفته المعينة ، لأجل أن الفاعل المختار ، خصصه بتلك الصفة من غير مرجح اصلا . فنقول : إذا جوزتم هذا ، فجوزوا أيضاً أن يخلق تلك المعجزة لا لمرجح أصلاً ، وذلك يقدح في قولنا إنه إنما خلق المعجز لأجل التصديق . وإذا ثبت هذا فحينئذ يخرج المعجز من أن يكون دليلاً على الصدق . فالحاصل : أن اختصاص كل جسم بصفته المعينة. إن كان على سبيل الوجوب أو على سبيل الأولوية ، امتنع حصول انخراق العادات ، فتبطل المعجزات . وإن كان لا على سبيل [ الوجوب ولا على سبيل ] الأولوية ، فذلك يقدح في كون المعجز دليلا على الصدق . والله أعلم
The second aspect in explaining that this is impossible: we say: The specification of every body with its specific attribute is either because its specification with that attribute is necessary, or if it is not necessary but it is more suitable than others, or this necessity and this suitability have not occurred. If it occurs with necessity, it is impossible; and if it occurs with suitability, then that suitability: if it is for the essence itself, its removal is impossible; and if its occurrence is due to another attribute, then the discussion about the second is like that about the first, so it follows that the occurrence of the suitability of every attribute is due to the precedence of another attribute without end. That necessitates the statement of the pre-eternity of the world, and this invalidates prophethood. As for the third division: it is that it is said: the specification of every body with its specific attribute is because the Free Agent specified it with that attribute without any preponderating factor at all. We say: If you permit this, then also permit that He create that miracle without any preponderating factor at all, and that undermines our statement that He only created the miracle for the sake of confirmation. When this is established, then the miracle ceases to be evidence for truthfulness. The conclusion: that the specification of every body with its specific attribute: if it is by way of necessity or by way of suitability, the occurrence of the violation of customs is impossible, so miracles are invalidated. And if it is not by way of necessity nor by way of suitability, that undermines the miracle being evidence for truthfulness. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (al-wajh al-thānī) to prove that violation of natural laws (inkhirāq al-ʿādāt) is impossible. The author uses a logical division: every body's specific attribute is either (1) necessary for it, (2) more suitable (awlā) than others, or (3) neither necessary nor suitable. If the attribute is necessary, change is impossible. If it is more suitable, that suitability either belongs to the essence itself (then removal is impossible) or is due to another attribute, leading to an infinite regress which implies the pre-eternity of the world and thus denies prophethood. The third possibility, that the Free Agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār) specifies without any preponderating factor (murjīḥ), would mean that miracles also occur without a reason, undermining their evidential value for confirming prophethood. The conclusion: if attributes are necessary or suitable, miracles (as violations of custom) cannot occur; if attributes are arbitrary, then miracles lose their role as evidence of truthfulness. Thus, both alternatives invalidate miracles as proofs of prophethood, as the author aims to show.

Key Terms
اختصاص كل جسم بصفته المعينة
وجوب
أولوية
مرجح
الفاعل المختار
انخراق العادات
المعجز
الصدق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Razi is denying the possibility of miracles altogether.
Correction: He is not denying miracles, but showing that if natural attributes are arbitrary, miracles lose their evidential power, and if they are necessary or suitable, miracles cannot occur. This is a critique of a specific philosophical position.

Checkpoint
According to the second aspect, what is the problem with the third division (specification by the Free Agent without any preponderating factor)?
A) It leads to infinite regress.
B) It necessitates the pre-eternity of the world.
C) It would then be possible for miracles to be created without any reason, thus they cannot serve as evidence for truthfulness.
D) It makes the attributes necessary.

Key Takeaway
The specification of attributes either makes miracles impossible or destroys their evidential value.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If miracles cannot serve as certain proof, what foundation remains for your trust in the truthfulness of the prophets? Are you willing to let go of the demand for absolute logical certainty and embrace faith as a risk?

Astrological Claims as an Alternative for Miracles
Presentation of astrological claims as a possible explanation for miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the astrological theory that some use to explain miracles, including the roles of celestial bodies, conjunctions, fixed stars, and the arrows of happiness and the unseen.

Core Idea
Astrology offers a naturalistic alternative to divine miracles.

In this passage, the author presents the fifth possible objection to the divine origin of miracles: astrological configurations. Philosophers and astrologers claim that celestial bodies and planetary conjunctions have specific influences on earthly events. The author lists various astrological concepts such as fixed stars, conjunctions, and the arrows of happiness and the unseen, which are said to grant knowledge and abilities. This sets the stage for the objection that maybe prophets' miracles stem from such astrological factors.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the technical terms used by the astrologers.
2
Identify the key astrological concepts
List the elements: celestial bodies, planetary conjunctions, fixed stars, arrows of happiness and the unseen. Understand what each is claimed to do.
3
Understand the claim of influence
The astrologers insist these have strong effects. Note that the author does not endorse this; he only reports it as a possible explanation.
This will be important for the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإحتمال الخامس: أن نقول: اتفقت الفلاسفة على أن للأجرام الفلكية، والاتصالات الكوكبية تأثيرات مخصوصة في أحوال هذا العالم، وقد اشتهر في ألسنة المنجمين: أن للكواكب الثابتة عطايا عظيمة في السعادة والنحوسة، والذكاء والبلادة، واتفقوا أيضاً على أن للقرانات آثاراً عظيمة في هذا الباب. واتفقوا على أنه لا تختلف أحوال تلك القرانات إلا وقوع الثوابت في البيوت المناسبة لها. واتفقوا على أن لسهم السعادة تأثيراً قوياً في إعطاء السعادات، ولسهم الغيب تأثيراً قوياً في إعطاء المعارف الحقة والوقوف على المغيبات. وعلماء الأحكام من الزمان الأقدم إلى عهدنا هذا، مصرون على صحة هذه الدعاوى، وجازمون بأن كل من جرب أحوال الطوالع، علم يقينا أن لهذه الأسباب آثاراً قوية في هذا الباب.
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: نحن لا ندعي صحة هذه الأصول، ولا أنها معلومة أو مظنونة، بل نقول: لا أقل من أن يكون احتمال أن يكون الأمر على ما قالوه قائماً. وبتقدير أن تصح كل هذه الأشياء أو بعضها. أن يكون اختصاص مدعي النبوة والرسالة بهذه المعجزات، إنما كان لأجل اشتمال طالع مولده على حالة من هذه الأحوال. فلعله وقع سهم السعادة وقوعاً عجيباً يقتضي حصول هذه السعادات. ولعله وقع سهم الغيب في طالعه وقوعاً يقتضي قدرته على الإخبار عن الغيوب.
وأنا أقول: إني قد رأيت إنساناً لم يتفق في طالع مولده شيء من الأشياء الكاملة، إلا أنه كانت الشعري اليمانية واقعة على درجة تاسعة. فلا جرم بلغ في العلوم النقلية والعقلية مبلغاً عالياً، من غير حاجة إلى تحمل تعب في المطالعة والتحصيل.
وإذا ثبت أن هذا الاحتمال قائم، ظهر أنه لا سبيل البتة إلى القطع بأن هذه الخوارق التي ظهرت على الأنبياء: من فعل الله تعالى.
وحكى محمد بن زكريا الرازي في بعض كتبه: أنه رأى رجلاً يهودياً، كان يستخرج الخبيء والضمير على أحسن الوجوه، وبقي على تلك الحالة
The fifth possibility: that we say: The philosophers have agreed that the celestial bodies and planetary conjunctions have specific influences on the conditions of this world, and it has been well known in the tongues of astrologers that the fixed stars have great gifts regarding happiness and misfortune, intelligence and dullness, and they also agreed that conjunctions have great effects in this regard. And they agreed that the conditions of those conjunctions do not differ except by the occurrence of the fixed stars in the houses appropriate for them. And they agreed that the arrow of happiness has a strong effect in granting happinesses, and the arrow of the unseen has a strong effect in granting true knowledge and knowledge of hidden things. And the scholars of judgments from ancient times to our present time persist in the soundness of these claims, and are certain that whoever experiments with the conditions of the ascendants knows for sure that these causes have strong effects in this regard.
If you know this, then we say: We do not claim the soundness of these principles, nor that they are known or conjectured, but we say: there is no less than that there is a possibility that the matter is as they said. And assuming that all these things or some of them are true, then the specification of the claimant of prophethood and messengership with these miracles would be only because his birth ascendant included a condition from these conditions. Perhaps the arrow of happiness occurred in a wondrous way necessitating the attainment of these happinesses. And perhaps the arrow of the unseen occurred in his ascendant in a way necessitating his ability to inform of unseen things.
And I say: I have seen a person who did not have any perfect thing in his birth ascendant, except that the star Sirius of Yemen was at the ninth degree. So inevitably he reached a high level in the transmitted and rational sciences, without need to endure hardship in study and acquisition.
And if it is established that this possibility exists, it becomes clear that there is no way at all to be certain that these marvels that appeared through the prophets are from the act of God Most High.
And Muhammad ibn Zakariyya al-Razi narrated in some of his books: that he saw a Jewish man who used to extract hidden things and consciences in the best manner, and he remained in that state...
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This lesson presents the fifth possible alternative explanation for miracles: astrological influences. The author first states that philosophers agree on the effects of celestial bodies and planetary conjunctions. Astrologers claim fixed stars grant happiness or misfortune, intelligence or dullness. Conjunctions have great effects, and their conditions depend on fixed stars in appropriate houses. Also, the 'arrow of happiness' and 'arrow of the unseen' have strong effects. The author notes that scholars of judgments (astrologers) insist on these claims. Then the author says he does not affirm these principles as true but insists that they are at least possible. If they are possible, then a prophet's miracles could be due to his birth chart having a favorable configuration, such as a strong 'arrow of happiness' or 'arrow of the unseen'. He gives an example: he saw a man who had no perfect aspects in his birth chart except that Sirius of Yemen was at the ninth degree, yet he achieved high levels in sciences without much effort. This supports the possibility. If this possibility stands, then we cannot be certain that miracles are from God. He then mentions a narration from Muhammad ibn Zakariyya al-Razi about a Jewish man who could extract hidden things and consciences, suggesting even a non-prophet could have such abilities, further undermining the certainty that miracles are divine.

Correction: The author is not affirming astrology; he is only presenting it as a possible explanation to challenge certainty about miracles.

?
The philosophers and astrologers claim that experience confirms their theories. Have you ever been tempted to trust your own experiences over divine revelation? When you are at a crossroads, do you consult the 'ascendants' of your own desires or submit to Allah's command?

Why Astrological Possibility Undermines Certainty about Miracles
Argument that astrology's possibility prevents certainty about miracles' divine origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mere possibility of astrological causation prevents us from being certain that miracles are from God.

Core Idea
If astrology might be true, we cannot be sure miracles are divine.

Having presented the astrological claims, the author now argues that even if we do not affirm their truth, their possibility is enough to cast doubt. He says that if the birth chart of a prophet happened to have favorable configurations, that could account for his miracles. He gives an anecdote of a man who achieved great knowledge despite having an imperfect chart except for one star, and relates a story from al-Razi about a Jewish man with extraordinary abilities. This suggests that non-prophets can have such powers, weakening the argument that miracles prove prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's cautious language: he does not claim the astrological principles are true, only possible.
2
Analyze the logical structure
The author first says the astrological claims are at least possible. Then he hypothesizes that if true, a prophet's birth chart could explain miracles. Then he provides evidence of non-prophets with similar abilities.
3
Understand the consequence
The conclusion: if this possibility exists, we cannot be certain that miracles are from God.
This is the key point of the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإحتمال الخامس: أن نقول: اتفقت الفلاسفة على أن للأجرام الفلكية، والاتصالات الكوكبية تأثيرات مخصوصة في أحوال هذا العالم، وقد اشتهر في ألسنة المنجمين: أن للكواكب الثابتة عطايا عظيمة في السعادة والنحوسة، والذكاء والبلادة، واتفقوا أيضاً على أن للقرانات آثاراً عظيمة في هذا الباب. واتفقوا على أنه لا تختلف أحوال تلك القرانات إلا وقوع الثوابت في البيوت المناسبة لها. واتفقوا على أن لسهم السعادة تأثيراً قوياً في إعطاء السعادات، ولسهم الغيب تأثيراً قوياً في إعطاء المعارف الحقة والوقوف على المغيبات. وعلماء الأحكام من الزمان الأقدم إلى عهدنا هذا، مصرون على صحة هذه الدعاوى، وجازمون بأن كل من جرب أحوال الطوالع، علم يقينا أن لهذه الأسباب آثاراً قوية في هذا الباب.
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: نحن لا ندعي صحة هذه الأصول، ولا أنها معلومة أو مظنونة، بل نقول: لا أقل من أن يكون احتمال أن يكون الأمر على ما قالوه قائماً. وبتقدير أن تصح كل هذه الأشياء أو بعضها. أن يكون اختصاص مدعي النبوة والرسالة بهذه المعجزات، إنما كان لأجل اشتمال طالع مولده على حالة من هذه الأحوال. فلعله وقع سهم السعادة وقوعاً عجيباً يقتضي حصول هذه السعادات. ولعله وقع سهم الغيب في طالعه وقوعاً يقتضي قدرته على الإخبار عن الغيوب.
وأنا أقول: إني قد رأيت إنساناً لم يتفق في طالع مولده شيء من الأشياء الكاملة، إلا أنه كانت الشعري اليمانية واقعة على درجة تاسعة. فلا جرم بلغ في العلوم النقلية والعقلية مبلغاً عالياً، من غير حاجة إلى تحمل تعب في المطالعة والتحصيل.
وإذا ثبت أن هذا الاحتمال قائم، ظهر أنه لا سبيل البتة إلى القطع بأن هذه الخوارق التي ظهرت على الأنبياء: من فعل الله تعالى.
وحكى محمد بن زكريا الرازي في بعض كتبه: أنه رأى رجلاً يهودياً، كان يستخرج الخبيء والضمير على أحسن الوجوه، وبقي على تلك الحالة
The fifth possibility: that we say: The philosophers have agreed that the celestial bodies and planetary conjunctions have specific influences on the conditions of this world, and it has been well known in the tongues of astrologers that the fixed stars have great gifts regarding happiness and misfortune, intelligence and dullness, and they also agreed that conjunctions have great effects in this regard. And they agreed that the conditions of those conjunctions do not differ except by the occurrence of the fixed stars in the houses appropriate for them. And they agreed that the arrow of happiness has a strong effect in granting happinesses, and the arrow of the unseen has a strong effect in granting true knowledge and knowledge of hidden things. And the scholars of judgments from ancient times to our present time persist in the soundness of these claims, and are certain that whoever experiments with the conditions of the ascendants knows for sure that these causes have strong effects in this regard.
If you know this, then we say: We do not claim the soundness of these principles, nor that they are known or conjectured, but we say: there is no less than that there is a possibility that the matter is as they said. And assuming that all these things or some of them are true, then the specification of the claimant of prophethood and messengership with these miracles would be only because his birth ascendant included a condition from these conditions. Perhaps the arrow of happiness occurred in a wondrous way necessitating the attainment of these happinesses. And perhaps the arrow of the unseen occurred in his ascendant in a way necessitating his ability to inform of unseen things.
And I say: I have seen a person who did not have any perfect thing in his birth ascendant, except that the star Sirius of Yemen was at the ninth degree. So inevitably he reached a high level in the transmitted and rational sciences, without need to endure hardship in study and acquisition.
And if it is established that this possibility exists, it becomes clear that there is no way at all to be certain that these marvels that appeared through the prophets are from the act of God Most High.
And Muhammad ibn Zakariyya al-Razi narrated in some of his books: that he saw a Jewish man who used to extract hidden things and consciences in the best manner, and he remained in that state...

The Sixth Possibility: Celestial Bodies as Miracle Agents
Author presents the sixth possibility and argues that the burden of proof lies on the one claiming impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth objection regarding celestial bodies as agents of miracles and the author's refutation based on burden of proof.

Core Idea
Even a mere possibility of other agents blocks certainty in divine miracles.

In this passage, the author presents a cunning objection: if celestial bodies are living, rational beings that can be invoked through rituals, then perhaps they, not God, produce miracles. The author does not simply dismiss it but shows why it cannot be refuted without proof. This lesson unpacks the objection and the author's strategy.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
Read the passage carefully and identify the claim of the philosophers and Sabians about celestial bodies.
They claim that celestial bodies are living, rational, and aware of earthly affairs.
2
See the invocation practice
Notice the description of how humans can invoke the spirit of a planet through specific rituals.
This is the basis for the possibility that planets could cause miracles.
3
Grasp the author's response
The author acknowledges that even if the claim is unproven, it remains a possibility that undermines certainty about God being the sole miracle agent.
The author does not need to prove the claim false here; he only needs to show that the possibility is not impossible.
4
Understand the burden of proof
Focus on the argument that the burden of proof is on the one claiming impossibility, not on the one raising a possibility.
This is a key dialectical move: the questioner does not need to prove his case; the respondent must show impossibility.
5
Distinguish from previous question
Note how this question differs from an earlier one: here celestial bodies are free agents, not necessitating causes.
This distinction shows the author's careful handling of different objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاحتمال السادس : اتفقت الفلاسفة والصائبة على أن الأفلاك والكواكب أحياء ناطقة ، مطلعة على جميع أحوال هذا العالم . وفي الناس من يدعي : أن الرجل إذا واظب على قراءة رقي مخصوصة أياماً مخصوصة على شرائط مخصوصة ، فإنه يتجلى له روح ذلك الكوكب ، ويعينه على مقاصده وأغراضه . وكتب أصحاب الطلسمات في دعوة الكواكب مملوءة من هذه الكلمات . إذا عرفت هذا الكلام فنقول : إنه وإن لم يثبت بالدليل صحة ما ذكروه ، إلا أنه لا أقل من الاحتمال . ومع قيام هذا الاحتمال ، لا يمكن القطع بأن خالق المعجزات هو الله تعالى . بل لا يمتنع أن يكون فاعلها هو هذه الكواكب ، ومن نظر في كتب السحر والطلمسات ، رأى حكايات عجيبة في هذا الباب . وكتاب تنكلوشا ، كتاب مشهور ، موجود في أيدي الناس ، وفيه من هذا الباب شيء كثير ، وقول من يقول : إنه من باب الخرافات كلام ما به بأس ، إلا أنه لا يدفع السؤال . لأن السائل لا حاجة به إلى إقامة الدليل على صحة ما ذكره في السؤال . وإنما المجيب هو المحتاج إلى إقامة الدلالة على أن ذلك الوجه محال باطل قطعاً . واعلم أن الفرق بين هذا السؤال ، وبين ما قبله : أما في السؤال الأول . جعلنا الكواكب ، موجبة بالذات ، لهذه الآثار العجيبة ، بحسب الشرائط المختلفة ، والأشكال الفلكية المتعاقبة . وأما في هذا السؤال . فقد جعلنا الأفلاك والكواكب ، أحياء ناطقة .. مختارة في الفعل والترك .
The sixth possibility: The philosophers and the Sabians agreed that the celestial spheres and planets are living, rational beings, informed of all states of this world. And among people there are those who claim that if a man persists in reading specific incantations on specific days under specific conditions, then the spirit of that planet will appear to him and assist him in his aims and purposes. The books of the talisman practitioners in invoking the planets are filled with these words. If you understand this statement, we say: Even if the truth of what they mentioned is not established by proof, there is still at least possibility. And with the existence of this possibility, it is impossible to be certain that the creator of miracles is God Almighty. Rather, it is not inconceivable that their agent is these planets. And whoever looks at the books of magic and talismans will see astonishing stories in this chapter. And the book Tinkalusha is a famous book, available in people's hands, and it contains many things of this chapter. And the statement of whoever says that it is a matter of superstitions is an acceptable statement, except that it does not repel the question. Because the questioner has no need to provide proof of the truth of what he mentioned in the question. Rather, the respondent is the one who needs to provide evidence that that aspect is impossible and definitively false. And know that the difference between this question and the one before it: As for the first question, we made the planets necessary by essence for these wondrous effects, according to different conditions and successive celestial configurations. And as for this question, we have made the spheres and planets living, rational beings, choosing in action and refraining.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth possibility that the author raises as an objection to the certainty that miracles come from God. It is a claim from philosophers and Sabians that celestial bodies are alive, rational, and aware of earthly affairs, and that through specific rituals one can gain their assistance. The author responds by acknowledging that even if this claim is not proven, it remains a possibility, and as long as it is possible, one cannot be certain that God is the sole agent of miracles. The author further defends the objection by noting that the burden of proof lies on the respondent to show impossibility, not on the questioner to prove their claim. Finally, the author distinguishes this question from a previous one: earlier the celestial bodies were assumed to be necessitating causes, while here they are free agents. This distinction is crucial for the argumentative structure.

Correction: The author is raising this as an objection to be refuted, not as his own belief. He only acknowledges it as a possibility to highlight the burden of proof.

The Seventh Possibility: Intellects and Souls as Miracle Agents
Author presents the seventh possibility that intellects and souls cause miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical claim that intellects and souls could be agents of miracles, and how the author distinguishes this from the theological concept of angels.

Core Idea
Intellects and souls are the philosophers' alternative to angels as miracle agents.

The author presents yet another hypothesis: what if miracles are caused by the intellects and souls that philosophers affirm? He notes that as long as their existence is not disproven, the possibility stands. He then clarifies why this question is distinct from the earlier one about angels: theologians see angels as different entities, so each objection is tailored to its audience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Note the structure: a possibility stated, a condition, a question, and a distinction.
The passage is short but dense with dialectical nuance.
2
Identify the core claim and condition
The core claim is that intellects and souls could be the agents of miracles. The condition is that this possibility remains as long as their existence is not disproven.
3
Understand the distinction from the angel question
The author distinguishes this possibility from the earlier question about angels because angels (in theology) are different from the intellects and souls of philosophy. Each question is tailored to the worldview of the opponent.
4
Reflect on the dialectical method
Notice how the author adapts his argument to his audience: he uses premises accepted by the philosophers. This shows the importance of arguing according to the opponent's framework in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاحتمال السابع : اتفقت الفلاسفة على إثبات العقول والنفوس . فقبل إقامة الدلالة على إبطال القول بها ، كان احتمال وجودها قائماً . وعلى هذا التقدير ، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون فاعل هذه المعجزات هو هذه العقول والنفوس ؟ والفرق بين هذا السؤال وبين سؤال الملائكة : هو أن الملائكة عند المتكلمين شيء مغاير لهذه العقول والنفوس ، فأوردنا سؤال الملائكة على حسب ما يعتقدون في وجود الملائكة وأوردنا هذا السؤال ههنا على حسب مذاهب الفلاسفة في العقول والنفوس .
And the seventh possibility: The philosophers agreed on affirming the intellects and souls. So before providing evidence to refute the claim to them, the possibility of their existence remains. And on this assumption, why is it not permissible that the agent of these miracles is these intellects and souls? And the difference between this question and the question about angels: it is that angels according to the theologians (mutakallimun) are something different from these intellects and souls. So we presented the question of angels according to what they believe about the existence of angels, and we presented this question here according to the doctrines of the philosophers about the intellects and souls.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh possibility, which is that the immaterial intellects and souls postulated by philosophers could be the agents of miracles. The author notes that as long as their existence is not proven impossible, the possibility stands. He then distinguishes this from the earlier question about angels: angels, in the view of the theologians, are entities distinct from the philosophical intellects and souls. Thus, the author tailored each objection to the worldview of the interlocutor—the angel question for theologians, and the intellect/soul question for philosophers. This shows the author's dialectical method of arguing according to the opponent's premises.

The author presents the possibility that intellects and souls (as per philosophers) could be miracle agents, and distinguishes this from the theological view of angels to address each school on its own terms.

Argument from Hylomorphism and Infinite Regress
Author presents an argument from hylomorphism yielding an infinite regress of preparednesses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a hylomorphic analysis of bodies leads to an infinite regress of preparednesses, and why this is considered impossible in classical kalam.

Core Idea
Specific attributes of bodies cannot be explained without infinite regress, which is impossible — so body must depend on something beyond itself.

In this passage, the author presents a sophisticated argument that begins with a simple observation: all elemental bodies share the same underlying matter. Yet each body has its own unique form and attributes. How can this be? The author explores two possibilities and shows that both lead to absurdities, ultimately challenging the self-sufficiency of the natural world.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise about matter
Read the first part: all bodies share matter (hayula) which is receptive to all forms. This common substrate is the starting point.
2
Understand the dilemma
The author poses two possible explanations for a body's specific attributes: either it had priority over other bodies, or not. Consider why the second option is dismissed.
The dismissal uses the principle of preponderance without a preponderator.
3
Trace the infinite regress
Once priority is accepted, that priority (preparedness) itself must be caused by a prior preparedness, ad infinitum. Notice how this creates a chain.
4
Reflect on the impossibility
The author argues that this infinite regress is impossible whether all preparednesses occur at once (simultaneous infinite causes) or in succession (infinite causal chain). Consider why both are rejected in kalam.
The passage cuts off before the final conclusion, but you can infer the argument's direction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاحتمال الثامن : أن نقول : لا شك أن أجسام هذا العالم العنصري ، مشتركة في الهيولى. وتلك الهيولى قابلة لجميع الصور والأعراض على البدن وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : اختصاص كل واحد من هذه الأجسام بصفته المعينة وصورته المعينة ، إما أن يكون لأجل أن ذلك الجسم كان أولى بقبول تلك الصفة والصورة من سائر الأجسام أو لم يكن كذلك . والثاني باطل . وإلا لزم أن يكون اختصاص ذلك الجسم بتلك الصفة مع كونه مساوياً لسائر الأجسام في القبول ، وفي عدم الأولوية ، يكون رجحانا ، لأحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر ، لا لمرجح ، وهو محال . ولما بطل هذا القسم ، ثبت أن القسم الأول حق . فنقول : واختصاص ذلك الجسم بذلك الاستعداد الخاص ، لا بد وأن يكون لأجل استعداد آخر ؛ إلى غير النهاية ، فتلك الاستعدادات إن حصلت دفعة واحدة ، لزم حصول أسباب ومسببات لا نهاية لها ، دفعة واحدة . وهو محال ، وإن حصلت على سبيل التعاقب ، وهو أن يكون كل استعداد سابق ، علة الحصول الاستعداد اللاحق ، فحينئذ يكون حصول هذا
The eighth possibility: We say: There is no doubt that the bodies of this elemental world share in matter (hayula). And that matter is receptive to all forms and accidents with the body. And if this is established, we say: The specification of each of these bodies with its particular attribute and its particular form is either because that body was more deserving of accepting that attribute and form than the rest of the bodies, or it was not so. The second is false. Otherwise it would follow that the specification of that body with that attribute, while being equal to the rest of the bodies in receptivity, and in the absence of priority, would be a preponderance of one side of a possible over another without a preponderator, which is impossible. And when this division is falsified, it is established that the first division is true. We say: And the specification of that body with that particular preparedness must be due to another preparedness, ad infinitum. So if these preparednesses occur all at once, it would entail the simultaneous occurrence of causes and effects without end, which is impossible. And if they occur in succession, each preceding preparedness being the cause of the succeeding preparedness, then this occurrence becomes...
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth possibility, which is an argument based on the hylomorphic composition of bodies. The author starts from the premise that all elemental bodies share the same underlying matter (hayula), which is receptive to all forms and accidents. The question then is: why does a particular body have its specific attribute and form? Two possibilities are considered: either that body has a priority over others in receiving that attribute, or it does not. The second alternative is dismissed because if all bodies are equally receptive and no priority exists, then the specific attribution would be an unjustified preponderance (tarjih) of one possibility over another without a preponderator (murajjih), which is impossible. Therefore, the first alternative is accepted: the body has a specific preparedness (isti‘dad) for that attribute. But then this preparedness itself must be due to a prior preparedness, leading to an infinite regress. This regress is then shown to be impossible whether the preparednesses occur simultaneously or successively, as both lead to absurdities. The text breaks off before the conclusion, but the logical structure is clear: the specific attribute of a body cannot be explained without invoking an infinite chain of preparednesses, which is impossible, thus the original assumption (that miracles come from these bodies) is undermined.

Key Terms
الهيولى
الصور
الأعراض
الاستعداد
الرجحان
المرجح
العلة
التعاقب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about the impossibility of infinite regress per se.
Correction: The argument focuses on the impossibility of an actual infinite regress of simultaneous causes, not on the concept of infinite regress in general.

Checkpoint
What is the first step in the author's reasoning in the eighth possibility?
A) Bodies are made of different types of matter.
B) All elemental bodies share the same underlying matter.
C) Each body has its own unique matter.
D) Forms and accidents are not real.

Key Takeaway
The specific attribute of a body cannot be explained without invoking an infinite regress of preparednesses, which is impossible, thus showing that the body's form must derive from something beyond itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The argument shows that without a sufficient cause, specification is arbitrary. In your own life, do you live as if your circumstances are random, or do you seek the purpose behind them? What would it mean for your daily choices if you saw each moment as divinely specified?
?
If an infinite regress of causes is impossible, then there must be an ultimate source of all specificity. Are you living as if that source is real, or are you acting as if the world is self-sufficient? What one change would align your life more with the reality of a Creator who gives form and purpose?

Knowledge and Impossibility of Circular Causality
First general argument: knowledge and causal circularity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the perfect correlation between God's knowledge and the known proves that correlation does not imply causation, because circular causality is impossible.

Core Idea
Correlation does not imply causation when circularity would result.

This lesson presents the first general argument against the claim that correlation indicates causation. It focuses on God's knowledge: since God's knowledge perfectly correlates with the existence of known things, if this correlation implied causation, we would fall into an impossible circularity. The argument shows that knowledge cannot be a cause of the known, nor the known a cause of knowledge, even though they are always found together.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing. Note how the author first establishes the necessary correlation between divine knowledge and the known, then argues against causation in both directions.
Pay attention to the term 'دور' (circularity).
2
Identify the two sub-arguments
The passage argues separately that knowledge cannot cause the known, and the known cannot cause knowledge. Find each sub-argument and understand the reasoning.
The first sub-argument uses circularity; the second is stated as 'apparent'.
3
Reflect on the concept of circularity
Think about why circular causality is impossible. How does this refute the idea that correlation implies causation?
This is the key logical insight of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إن علم الله تعالى بالشيء المعين ، واجب الحصول مع حصول ذلك الشيء المعين ، لأنه لما ثبت أنه تعالى يجب كونه عالماً بجميع المعلومات ، فعلى أي وجه يقع ذلك المعلوم ، يكون العلم متعلقاً به ، كما هو عليه في نفسه . ثم إن العلم يمتنع أن يكون علة للمعلوم وبالعكس . أما أن العلم يمتنع أن يكون علة للمعلوم ، فلأن العلم بالشيء ، تابع لذلك المعلوم ، فلو كان المعلوم معللاً به ، لزم كون المعلوم تابعاً للعلم . وهو دور ، والدور محال . وأما أن المعلوم يمتنع أن يكون علة للعلم ، فهو ظاهر . فقد ثبت أن الدوران حاصل قطعاً مع علم الله تعالى بجميع المعلومات ، مع أنه يمتنع أن يكون واحد منهما علة للآخر .
First: The knowledge of God the Exalted about a specific thing is necessarily obtained with the occurrence of that specific thing, because when it has been established that He the Exalted must be knowing all known things, then in whatever way that known thing occurs, knowledge is related to it as it is in itself. Then knowledge is impossible to be a cause of the known and vice versa. As for the impossibility of knowledge being a cause of the known, it is because knowledge of a thing follows that known thing; so if the known were caused by it, it would necessitate that the known follows knowledge, which is a circularity, and circularity is impossible. And as for the impossibility of the known being a cause of knowledge, it is apparent. Thus it is established that correlation (دوران) definitely occurs with God's knowledge of all known things, while it is impossible for either of them to be a cause of the other.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents the first general argument (وجه) against the claim that correlation indicates causation. The argument focuses on God's knowledge. The author states that God's knowledge of a specific thing necessarily coincides with the existence of that thing. Since God knows all things, whenever a known thing occurs, God's knowledge of it is present. Then the author argues that knowledge cannot be a cause of the known, because knowledge follows the known (it is a consequence). If knowledge were a cause, it would lead to circularity where the known follows knowledge and knowledge follows the known, which is impossible. Similarly, the known cannot be a cause of knowledge, which is obvious. Therefore, despite the perfect correlation between God's knowledge and the known, neither is the cause of the other. This shows that correlation alone does not prove causation.

Key Terms
علم الله
علة
معلوم
دور
دوران

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that whenever two things are always found together, one must be the cause of the other.
Correction: This argument shows that if the two things are perfectly correlated but allowing causation would lead to circularity, then causation is impossible. Correlation alone is not sufficient proof of causation.

Checkpoint
What logical fallacy does the first argument use to disprove that correlation implies causation?
A) Infinite regress
B) Circularity (دور)
C) False analogy
D) Begging the question

Key Takeaway
Perfect correlation between two things does not prove causation when it leads to circularity, as shown by God's knowledge and the known.

Will you repent from assuming you can manipulate Allah through works?

Second General Argument: Equality of Cause and Effect
Second general argument: cause and effect may be equal or unequal in generality; correlation alone does not indicate causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why correlation between cause and effect does not necessarily indicate a causal relationship, using the examples of equal and more general effects.

Core Idea
Correlation alone does not prove causality.

The second general argument addresses a key weakness in using observed correlations to infer causal relationships. The author presents two scenarios: when cause and effect are co-extensive, and when the effect is more general. Through these examples, he demonstrates that perfect correlation can exist without a one-way causal link. Let's examine the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice the two examples of cause-effect relationships.
2
Identify the two cases
The passage presents two scenarios: (1) cause and effect are equal in generality (sunrise and daytime), and (2) effect is more general than cause (fever with multiple causes). Understand each example.
3
Follow the reasoning
The argument uses these examples to show that even when two things are perfectly correlated (both exist and cease together), causality is not established. The fever example shows that a single effect can have multiple possible causes.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The passage concludes that correlation (دوران) does not indicate causality. Reflect on why this is important for theological arguments about the existence of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن المعلول قد يكون مساوياً للعلة ، وقد يكون أعم منها . أما الأول فمثل قولنا : طلوع الشمس علة لوجود النهار ، ووجود النهار أيضاً لا يحصل إلا عند طلوع الشمس . فههنا العلة والمعلول متساويان . وأما الثاني . فمثل الحمى . فإنه قد يكون لعفونة الخلط ، وقد تكون لسبب آخر . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : العلة والمعلول إذا كانا متساويين في العموم والخصوص ، فكل واحد منهما دائر مع الآخر وجوداً ، وعدماً . والعلية والمعلولية غير مشتركة من الجانبين. فثبت أن الدوران لا يدل على العلية .
Second: The effect may be equal to the cause, or it may be more general than it. As for the first, it is like our statement: 'The rising of the sun is the cause of the existence of daytime, and daytime does not occur except at sunrise.' Here the cause and effect are equal. As for the second, it is like fever, for it may be due to putrefaction of a humor, or it may be due to another cause. When this is established, we say: If cause and effect are equal in generality and specificity, then each of them coexists with the other in existence and non-existence. But causality and effecthood are not shared from both sides. Thus it is established that correlation does not indicate causality.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This second argument addresses the relationship of equality in extension between cause and effect. The author points out that an effect can be equal to its cause (like daytime and sunrise) or more general (like fever, which has multiple causes). When cause and effect are co-extensive, they are correlated perfectly. However, causality is a one-way relationship, not shared. The example of fever shows that even if something is always present with a cause, it may not be the effect of that cause alone. The argument concludes that correlation does not prove causality, because even if two things are always together, we cannot determine which is cause and which is effect, or if they have a common cause.

Key Terms
علة
معلول
مساو
أعم
دوران

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things always occur together, one must cause the other.
Correction: The fever example shows that a single effect can have multiple possible causes, so perfect correlation does not guarantee causality.

Checkpoint
The second argument uses the example of fever to show that an effect can be:
A) equal to its cause
B) more general than its cause
C) less general than its cause
D) unrelated to its cause

Key Takeaway
Correlation alone does not prove causality; an effect can have multiple causes or be co-extensive with its cause.

Why Correlation Cannot Prove Causality
Third general argument: correlation is a common factor between cause and non-cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why correlation (dawaran) is considered a common factor that cannot serve as a specific proof of causality.

Core Idea
Correlation is a common factor shared by causes and non-causes, not a unique sign of causation.

This third and final general argument against using correlation as a proof of causality focuses on the logical nature of what is correlated. The author argues that the correlated thing must have its own constitutive differentia, making correlation a shared feature rather than a unique indicator. This passage concludes the series of general arguments against correlation as a proof of causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic once for overall meaning, then read the translation. Notice the key terms 'فصل مقوم' and 'خاصية'.
The passage is short but dense with logical terminology.
2
Identify the key logical move
The argument states that the correlated thing must have its own constitutive differentia, but correlation is a common factor shared by cause and non-cause. Note how this undermines the use of correlation as proof.
The move is from the nature of the correlated thing to the sharedness of correlation.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that these are general aspects showing correlation does not indicate causality at all. Consider why the author emphasizes 'at all' (البتة).
This is a strong rejection of any evidential value of correlation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن الشيء الذي يدور مع غيره وجوداً وعدماً ، فإنه لا بد وأن فصله المقوم لماهيته، ومع خاصيته المساوية لماهيته ، فالدوران قدر مشترك بين العلة وبين غيرها ، فيمتنع كونه دليلاً على العلية بعينها . فهذه وجوه إجمالية دالة على أن الدوران لا يدل على العلة البتة .
Third: A thing that coexists with another in existence and non-existence must have its constitutive differentia (فصل مقوم) and its property equal to its essence. So correlation is a common factor between the cause and other than it, thus it is impossible for it to be a proof of causality per se. These are general aspects indicating that correlation does not indicate causality at all.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This third argument focuses on the nature of the thing that is correlated. The author states that correlation is something shared by the cause and by non-causes. In logic, to establish causality, one needs a specific distinguishing characteristic (فصل مقوم) that is unique to the causal relation. Since correlation is a common feature, it cannot be the specific proof of causality. The author then concludes the three general arguments by affirming that correlation does not indicate causality at all.

Key Terms
فصل مقوم
خاصية
دوران
علية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Constant conjunction is sufficient to prove causation.
Correction: Correlation is a common factor shared by causes and non-causes; it does not uniquely indicate causality.

Checkpoint

B) It is a common factor between causes and non-causes

D) It is unique to causes

Key Takeaway
Correlation cannot prove causality because it is a common factor shared by causes and non-causes, not a unique distinguishing feature.
