Three Alternative Explanations for a King's Action
Three examples showing alternative motives for a king's action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a coincidental action by a king (or angel) can have multiple plausible explanations, not just confirming a claimant, and that this undermines the claim of certainty.

Core Idea
Coincidence is not proof: three alternative motives can explain the same action.

The detailed argument presents three alternative reasons why a king might perform an action coinciding with a claimant's request. By imagining plausible scenarios—like a snake falling on his garment, wanting to see something, or acting in anger—the author shows that the inference of confirmation is weak. This passage helps us see the logical fallacy of assuming a single cause for a correlated event.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the three numbered examples (الأول, الثاني, الثالث).
2
Identify the three alternatives
List each alternative explanation the author gives for the king's action. Note the specific motive in each case: snake/scorpion, seeing something, anger/mockery.
Consider how these are all mundane reasons unrelated to confirming the claimant.
3
Understand the weak impression vs certainty
Notice that the author admits a weak impression (إيهاما ضعيفا) but firmly denies that this yields certainty (قطع). Reflect on why this distinction matters for evaluating evidence.
This is the key logical move: correlation does not equal causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما التفصيل : فبيانه : أنه لا يبعد في العقل أن ذلك الملك إنما أتى بذلك الفعل في ذلك الوقت ، لأغراض أخرى ، سوى تصديق ذلك الرجل . وبيانه من وجوه : الأول : إن ذلك الملك لعله وقع على ثوبه في تلك الساعة ، حية أو عقرب . فلأجل احترازه عنه ، قام ذلك الملك ، لا لغرض تصديق المدعي . والثاني : لعله لاح له من البعد شيء ، احتاج إلى معرفته ، فقام ليتمكن من رؤيته كما ينبغي . الثالث : لعله قام غضباً على ذلك القائل المدعي ، أو استهزاء به ، ويكون مقصوده : أنه وإن فعل ما التمسه منه ذلك الطالب ، لكنه لا يلتفت إليه ، ولا يقيم له وزناً . نعم لا ينكر أن صدور ذلك الفعل من ذلك الملك في ذلك الوقت على وفق دعوى المدعي ، يوهم إيهاماً ضعيفاً : أن الغرض هو تصديق ذلك المدعي . فأما أن يقال : إنه يفيد القطع والجزم بأنه لا غرض له فيه إلا هذا التصديق : فبعيد .
As for the detailed [argument], its explanation is that it is not far-fetched in reason that that king (or angel) performed that action at that time for other purposes besides confirming that man's truth. Its explanation is from several aspects: First: Perhaps a snake or scorpion fell on his garment at that hour. So in order to avoid it, that king stood up, not for the purpose of confirming the claimant's truth. Second: Perhaps a thing appeared to him from a distance that he needed to know, so he stood up to be able to see it properly. Third: Perhaps he stood up in anger at that speaker claimant, or in mockery of him, and his intention was that even though he did what that petitioner asked of him, he does not pay attention to him or give him any weight. Yes, it is not denied that the occurrence of that action from that king at that time in accordance with the claimant's claim gives a weak impression that the purpose is confirming that claimant. But as for saying that it yields certainty and conviction that he has no purpose other than this confirmation, that is far-fetched.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the detailed (تفصيلي) argument. The author introduces a scenario: a king (or angel) performs an action at the same time that a claimant makes a request, suggesting that the action is a confirmation of the claimant's truth. The detailed argument proposes alternative explanations for the king's action that are not related to confirming the claimant. Three possibilities are given: 1) The king might have stood up because a snake or scorpion fell on his garment, to avoid it. 2) He might have stood to see something distant more clearly. 3) He might have stood up in anger or to mock the claimant, showing disregard. The author concedes that the coincidence gives a weak impression of confirmation, but denies that it yields certainty. Thus, the correlation does not prove that the motive was confirmation.

Key Terms
تفصيل
ملك
مدعي
غرض
قطع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A coincidental action matching a request is strong proof that the action was intended as a confirmation.
Correction: The author shows that many alternative motives exist, so the coincidence gives only a weak impression, not certainty.

Checkpoint
What is the weakest impression conceded by the author in the detailed argument?
A) The king's action might be a coincidence
B) The action gives a weak impression of confirmation
C) The king definitely did not confirm
D) The king's action proves the claimant's truth

Key Takeaway
The coincidence of an action with a request does not prove the action's purpose, as multiple alternative motives are rationally possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Why the Human King Analogy Fails for God
Second supposition: conceding the human case, questioning its application to God, first difference given.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first critical difference between a human king and God that undermines the analogy used by proponents of the argument from miracles.

Core Idea
Human kings act from self-interest; God is beyond such motives.

The opponent concedes the argument works for human kings but questions why it must apply to God. The first clear difference emerges: our judgment that a king's action confirms a claimant depends on our knowledge that the king cares about his kingdom. If the king were indifferent, we would not make that inference. This distinction challenges the analogy's extension to God.

Guided Path
1
Accept the premise, question the leap
Read the opening statement where the opponent accepts the argument for human kings but asks why it must apply to God. Notice the shift from observed world (shahid) to divine realm (gha'ib).
This is a common tactic in kalam: conceding a point to test its universal applicability.
2
Identify the condition for inference
The passage states that we judge the king to be confirming the claimant only when we know that the king cares for his kingdom's interests. Find that condition in the text.
The condition is 'when we know he cares for the interests of his kingdom'.
3
See how indifference breaks the analogy
If the king were indifferent to interests and harms, the presumption would not occur. Reflect on why this is significant: God is not motivated by self-interest, so the analogy fails.
This step is the core of the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والافتراض الثاني : أن نقول : سلمنا إنه في الشاهد كما ذكرتم . فلم قلتم : إنه يجب أن يكون في حق الله تعالى كذلك ؟ والفرق من وجوه : الأول : إنا إنما قضينا على ذلك الملك بكونه مصدقاً لذلك المدعي في دعواه . إذا عرفنا أنه يراعي مصالح ملكه ، وأنه لا يفعل فعلا تتشوش بسيبه مملكته. أما إذا اعتقدنا في ذلك الملك : أنه لا يبالي بالمصالح والمفاسد البتة ، فإنه لا يحصل هذا الظن ، أو إن اعتقدنا فيه أنه يراعي المصالح والمفاسد
And the second supposition: that we say: We accept that it is so in the observed world (الشاهد) as you have stated. But why do you say that it must be likewise concerning God the Exalted? And the difference is from several aspects: First: We only judged that king to be confirming that claimant in his claim when we know that he cares for the interests of his kingdom, and that he does not do an action that would disturb his kingdom. But if we believed concerning that king that he does not care at all about interests and harms, then this presumption would not occur, or if we believed that he does care about interests and harms...
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This is the second supposition (الافتراض الثاني), which presents an objection: even if the argument about human kings is accepted, why must it apply to God? The author then gives the first difference: Our judgment that the king's action was a confirmation is based on our knowledge that he cares for his kingdom's interests. If the king were indifferent to interests, we would not presume that motive. This implies that God is not bound by such considerations, so the analogy fails. The passage cuts off here, but the point is that God's actions are not constrained by the same considerations as human kings.

Three Objections Against Using Coincidence (Rotation) as Proof of Causation
The author gives three reasons why a king's action may not indicate truthfulness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three plausible alternative explanations that undermine the argument that a coincidental action proves a specific cause, and why coincidence yields only a weak impression, not certainty.

Core Idea
Coincidence (rotation) gives only a weak impression, not certain proof of causation.

The author of this passage argues that just because a king's action coincides with a claimant's request, it does not prove the king intended to confirm the claimant. He provides three plausible alternative motives to show that the action could have been for other reasons. This passage teaches us to be cautious when inferring causes from mere coincidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own examples come through before interpreting.
Notice the author begins with general aspects and then gives detailed objections.
2
Identify the three objections
The author lists three specific reasons why the king might have stood up other than to confirm the claimant. Find each one and notice how each is introduced with 'perhaps' (لعل).
The objections are: 1) snake/scorpion, 2) seeing something in distance, 3) anger or mockery.
3
Reflect on the author's conclusion
After listing the objections, the author acknowledges that the action gives a weak impression of confirmation but denies it yields certainty. Ask yourself: why does the author stress that it is 'far' (بعيد) to claim certainty?
This connects to the lesson's core idea: coincidence alone does not indicate causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوه إجمالية دالة على أن الدوران لا يدل على العلة البتة. أما التفصيل: فبيانه: أنه لا يبعد في العقل أن ذلك الملك إنما أتى بذلك الفعل في ذلك الوقت، لأغراض أخرى، سوى تصديق ذلك الرجل. وبيانه من وجوه: الأول: إن ذلك الملك لعله وقع على ثوبه في تلك الساعة، حية أو عقرب. فلأجل احترازه عنه، قام ذلك الملك، لا لغرض تصديق المدعي. والثاني: لعله لاح له من البعد شيء، احتاج إلى معرفته، فقام ليتمكن من رؤيته كما ينبغي. الثالث: لعله قام غضباً على ذلك القائل المدعي، أو استهزاء به، ويكون مقصوده: أنه وإن فعل ما التمسه منه ذلك الطالب، لكنه لا يلتفت إليه، ولا يقيم له وزناً. نعم لا ينكر أن صدور ذلك الفعل من ذلك الملك في ذلك الوقت على وفق دعوى المدعي، يوهم إيهاماً ضعيفاً: أن الغرض هو تصديق ذلك المدعي. فأما أن يقال: إنه يفيد القطع والجزم بأنه لا غرض له فيه إلا هذا التصديق: فبعيد.
General aspects indicating that rotation does not indicate causation at all. As for the detail: its explanation is that it is not far in reason that that king only performed that action at that time for other purposes, besides confirming that man. And its explanation from aspects: First: Perhaps that king had a snake or scorpion fall on his garment at that hour. So to avoid it, that king stood up, not for the purpose of confirming the claimant. Second: Perhaps something appeared to him from a distance that he needed to know, so he stood up to be able to see it as it should be. Third: Perhaps he stood up in anger at that claimant, or in mockery of him, and his intention was that although he did what that seeker requested of him, he pays no attention to him and does not give him any weight. Yes, it cannot be denied that the occurrence of that act from that king at that time in accordance with the claim of the claimant gives a weak impression that the purpose is to confirm that claimant. As for saying that it yields certainty and assurance that he has no purpose in it except this confirmation: that is far.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage presents three detailed objections to the claim that a coincidental action (rotation) indicates a specific cause. The author begins by stating that there are general aspects proving that rotation does not indicate causation at all. Then he provides a detailed explanation: it is not unreasonable that the king performed the requested action for other reasons. He lists three possible alternative motives: (1) a snake or scorpion landing on his garment causing him to stand; (2) seeing something in the distance that required a better view; (3) standing in anger or mockery to show disregard. The author acknowledges that the action gives a weak impression of confirmation, but denies it yields certainty. Thus, the passage guards against the fallacy of taking a single concurrent event as definitive proof of a cause.

Proof That Only God Is the True Agent
Refutes the second and third divisions of the proof that only God is the agent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is impossible for two independent causes to produce the same effect and why only God, the Necessary Being, can be the true agent.

Core Idea
Two independent causes cannot both produce one effect; only God is the true agent.

This passage continues the proof that only God is the true agent by examining two problematic scenarios. It shows that if two independent causes were both fully capable, they would prevent each other, leading to contradiction. Then it refutes the idea that one of them alone produces the effect, using reason and the nature of possible existents.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario of two independent causes
Read the first part of the passage describing the second division (al-qism al-thani). Notice the argument that if each is fully capable alone, the presence of the other prevents influence, but that prevention leads to a contradiction.
2
Examine the refutation of the third division
The passage then refutes the idea that the effect comes from one cause only. Two reasons are given: the weaker is repelled by the stronger, and a single thing does not admit variation in power. Read carefully.
3
Connect to the conclusion
Finally, see how the proof concludes that all possible things are in God's power, and claiming another influencer leads to impossible divisions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : أن من المعلوم أن كل واحد من هذين السببين المستقلين ، لو انفرد ، لكان مستقلاً بالتكوين والتأثير، إلا أن حضور الآخر منعه من التأثير . فنقول : المانع لهذا المؤثر ، ليس هو ذات المؤثر الثاني ، ولا شيء من صفاته ، بل كون هذا الأثر صادراً من هذا يمنع صدوره من ذاك ، ولذاك بالضد . ومعلوم : أن المعلول لا يحصل إلا عند حصول العلة . فإذا كان المانع من صدور ذلك الأثر عن هذا ، ليس إلا صدوره عن ذاك ، والمانع من صدوره من ذاك ليس إلا صدوره عن هذا ، وثبت أن المعلول لا يوجد إلا عند وجود العلة ، فلو امتنع الطرفان ، لزم القول بحصول الطرفين ، حتى يكون كل واحد منهما مانعاً من الثاني ، فيلزم من امتناع استناده إليهما معاً ، حصول استناده إليهما معاً [ وكنا قد ذكرنا في القسم الأول : أنه يلزم من حصول استناده إليهما معاً ، امتناع استناده إليهما معاً ] وكل ذلك محال. وأما القسم الثالث وهو أن يقع بأحدهما دون الثاني : فنقول : هذا باطل لوجهين: الأول: إن العقل يقتضي أن يندفع الأضعف بالأقوى، ولا شك أن ممكن الوجود، أضعف من واجب الوجود. وعلى هذا التقدير فلا موجد ولا مؤثر إلا الله. الثاني: إن كل شيء يخرج من العدم إلى الوجود فهو في نفسه، إما شيء واحد وإما مركب من أشياء، يكون كل واحد منها في نفسه شيئاً واحداً، والشيء الواحد في نفسه لا يقبل التفاوت أصلاً، وإذا كان هو في نفسه غير قابل للتفاوت كانت القدرة على إيجاده غير قابلة للتفاوت أصلاً، وإذا كان كذلك امتنع أن يقال : إن أحد المؤثرين أقوى وأكمل في التأثير بالنسبة إلى ذلك الشيء الواحد. بلى قد يعقل كون أحدهما قادراً على أشياء سوي ذلك الواحد، لكن المطلوب ههنا بيان القدرة والقوة بالنسبة إلى ذلك الشيء الواحد لا تقبل التفاوت. وإذا حصل الاستواء بين هذين المؤثرين، كان القول بأن أحدهما أولى بالتأثير، موجباً رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح، وهو محال. فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن جميع الممكنات مقدورة الله تعالى. وثبت : أنه متى كان الأمر كذلك، كان القول بإثبات مؤثر غير الله تعالى؛ يفضي إلى هذه الأقسام الباطلة، فكان القول به محالاً، فثبت بهذا البرهان الكامل : أنه لا مؤثر في الوجود إلا الله أصلاً.
And the second: It is known that each one of these two independent causes, if it were alone, would be independent in originating and influencing, except that the presence of the other prevented it from influencing. We say: The preventer for this influencer is not the essence of the second influencer, nor any of its attributes, but rather the fact that this effect emanates from this one prevents its emanation from that one, and vice versa. It is known that the effect does not occur except when the cause occurs. Since the preventer of that effect from this one is only its emanation from that one, and the preventer of its emanation from that one is only its emanation from this one, and it is established that the effect does not exist except when the cause exists, then if both sides were prevented, it would necessitate saying that both sides occur, so that each one is a preventer of the second. Thus, from the prevention of its attribution to both together, its attribution to both together would follow, and we have already mentioned in the first section that from its attribution to both together, its prevention from both together follows, and all of that is impossible. As for the third section, which is that it occurs by one of them without the other, we say: This is false for two reasons: First: Reason requires that the weaker be repelled by the stronger, and there is no doubt that the possible existent is weaker than the necessary existent. On this estimation, there is no creator and no influencer except Allah. Second: Everything that comes out of non-existence into existence is, in itself, either one thing or composed of things, each of which is in itself one thing. The one thing in itself does not admit variation at all. If it is in itself not susceptible to variation, then the power to bring it into existence is not susceptible to variation at all. If that is so, it is impossible to say that one of the two influencers is stronger and more perfect in influencing with respect to that one thing. Yes, it might be understood that one of them is capable of things other than that one, but what is required here is to show that capability and power with respect to that one thing do not admit variation. If equality is obtained between these two influencers, then saying that one of them is more deserving of influence necessitates a preference of one side of the possible over the other without a preponderator, which is impossible. So it is established by what we have mentioned that all possible things are within the power of Allah the Exalted. And it is established that whenever the matter is so, the claim of establishing an influencer other than Allah the Exalted leads to these false divisions, so the claim of it is impossible. Thus it is established by this perfect proof that there is no influencer in existence except Allah fundamentally.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhān) that only God is the true agent (muʼaththir). It begins by examining the second scenario (al-qism al-thānī) in a previous division: two independent causes each fully capable on their own. If both were present, they would prevent each other. The prevention is not due to essence or attributes but because the effect's occurrence from one prevents it from occurring from the other. If both were prevented, they would both have to occur simultaneously to prevent each other, which is contradictory. Then the third scenario (al-qism al-thālith) is refuted: that the effect occurs from one of them alone. Two reasons: First, reason says the weaker (the possible) is repelled by the stronger (the necessary), so only God can be the creator. Second, any single thing that comes into existence is indivisible in its essence, so power over it cannot vary; thus one influencer cannot be stronger than another with respect to that single thing. An objection is answered: one influencer might be capable of more things, but for this specific single thing, power is equal, so preference without a preponderator is impossible. The proof then concludes that all possible things are in God's power, and claiming another influencer leads to these absurd divisions, so only God is the true agent.

Key Terms
مؤثر
إمكان
واجب الوجود
ممكن الوجود
العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof denies any causality in the natural world, meaning nothing in creation has any effect.
Correction: The proof is about ultimate agency; secondary causality is not denied, only that true independent agency belongs to God alone.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof in this passage, why is it impossible for two independent causes to both produce the same effect?
A) Because they would share the same essence.
B) Because each would prevent the other by the very fact that the effect proceeds from the other.
C) Because the effect would require a stronger cause.
D) Because the effect is composed of parts.

Key Takeaway
This proof shows that claiming a true agent other than God leads to logical contradictions, so only God is the true agent.

Is there any area of your life where you fear a created thing more than you fear God? What would it mean to truly believe that nothing has real influence except Him?

Second Proof: Possibility Necessitates a Specific Cause
Second proof: possibility as cause for need of a specific influencer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the principle of possibility leads to the conclusion that only God is the true agent.

Core Idea
Every possible thing depends on a specific, determinate cause.

This lesson presents the second proof from al-Matalib al-Aliyah that only God is the agent. It uses a logical disjunction: either possibility requires a specific cause or not. By eliminating the undetermined option, it establishes that every contingent being needs a specific cause, which must be the One.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to get the argument's flow.
Notice the compact logical style of Islamic kalam.
2
Identify the disjunctive structure
Find the two options: either the need is for a specific influencer or not. Understand why the author rejects the second.
The key phrase is 'faimmā an yakūna... aw lā bi'aynihi'.
3
Trace the refutation of the second option
The author argues that what is not determinate cannot exist, so it cannot be a cause. Follow the steps of the refutation.
This refutation relies on the principle that existence implies determinacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : إن الإمكان علة للحاجة إلى المؤثر . فإما أن يكون علة للحاجة إلى مؤثر بعينه أو لا بعينه ، والثاني باطل . لأن كل ما كان موجوداً في نفس الأمر ، فهو متعين في نفسه ، فما لا يكون متعيناً في نفسه امتنع كونه موجوداً في نفسه وما كان ممتنع الوجود ، امتنع أن يكون علة لوجود غيره ، ولما بطل هذا القسم ، بقي القسم الأول وهو أن الإمكان علة الحاجة إلى شيء بعينه ، فوجب أن يكون كل ممكن محتاجاً في وجوده إليه ، وإذا كان كذلك ، فلا مؤثر إلا الواحد . فهذا هو أحد الأصول الثلاثة التي لابد من معرفته في هذا الباب.
The second argument: Possibility is a cause of the need for the influencer. So either it is a cause of the need for a specific influencer or not specific, and the second is false. Because everything that exists in actual fact is determined in itself, so what is not determined in itself is impossible to exist in itself, and what is impossible in existence is impossible to be a cause for the existence of another. When this division is false, the first division remains, which is that possibility is a cause of the need for a specific thing, so it is necessary that every possible thing is in need in its existence of Him. If that is so, then there is no influencer except the One. So this is one of the three principles which must be known in this chapter.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof (al-ḥujja al-thāniya) that only God is the agent. It argues that possibility (al-imkān) is the reason for needing an influencer (muʼaththir). The proof is a disjunctive syllogism: either this need is for a specific influencer or not specific. The second option is false because anything that exists in reality is determinate in itself; what is indeterminate cannot exist, and what cannot exist cannot be a cause for another. Thus the need must be for a specific influencer. Therefore, every possible being depends on that specific one, who is no other than the One (Allah). The author then states that this constitutes one of the three principles necessary for this chapter (i.e., the first principle).

Key Terms
الإمكان
العلة
المؤثر
الواحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument implies that God is directly responsible for every event, including evil.
Correction: The proof only establishes that every possible being depends on God for existence; it does not address moral responsibility or the problem of evil.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, why is the possibility of a non-specific cause false?
A) Because possibility itself is indeterminate.
B) Because a non-specific cause would be impossible in itself.
C) Because the cause must be as determinate as its effect.
D) Because everything that exists is determinate, so its cause must be determinate.

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that every contingent being must have a specific cause, which is the One.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The proof says that what is not determinate cannot exist. Consider your own identity: are you living as someone whose existence is determined by God's will, or are you trying to be undefined and independent? How does this affect your obedience to Him?
?
If there is no influencer except the One, then every good you have comes directly from Him. Think of one blessing you have been attributing to your own effort or luck. How can you consciously redirect that gratitude to God alone, starting now?

The Three Possible Causes and the Possibility of Substitution
Enumerates three causal theories and argues substitution possible in all.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible causes of contingent events according to kalam and why the possibility of substitution holds in all three.

Core Idea
No matter the cause, replacement is always conceivable.

In this passage, the author systematically examines every possible explanation for how events occur—whether by chance, by a necessary cause, or by a free agent—and demonstrates that each case leaves room for the possibility that a person could be replaced by an identical counterpart in an instant. This argument is a key example of how kalam uses rational analysis to explore even far-fetched scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the full argument. Notice the three divisions the author presents.
2
Identify the three causal theories
The author lists three possibilities: no cause (chance), necessitating cause, and free cause. See if you can spot each one in the text.
The phrase 'ليس وراء هذه الأقسام الثلاثة قسم البتة' emphasizes exhaustiveness.
3
Follow the argument for each division
For each division, the author shows that substitution is possible. Note the reasoning: for chance, because if chance can cause events, it can cause substitution; for necessitating cause, due to celestial configurations; for free cause, because a free agent can directly choose to substitute.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that the possibility of substitution is established on all three estimations. Reflect on why this matters—it shows that rational possibility does not depend on the specific causal theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أولاً، مع أن التجويز القطعي قائم بكونه مغايراً لذلك الأول. وبيانه: أن الحادث إما أن لا يفتقر إلى المؤثر أو يفتقر إليه، والقسم الثاني فمؤثر العالم إما أن يكون موجباً أو مختاراً. وظاهر أنه ليس وراء هذه الأقسام الثلاثة قسم البتة. أما القسم الأول: وهو أن يقال: لا مؤثر لهذه الحوادث. فعلى هذا التقدير يكون الوجود بعد العدم، والعدم بعد الوجود: حاصلا بمحض الاتفاق من غير سبب أصلا. وإذا جاز ذلك، جاز أيضاً أن يقال: اتفق أن ذلك الشخص الأول قد عدم في تلك اللحظة اللطيفة، وحصل الشخص الثاني على سبيل الاتفاق. لأن القول بالاتفاق، لما كان واقعاً في بعض الصور، كان احتماله قائماً في كل الصور. وأما القسم الثاني: وهو أن يقال: مؤثر العالم موجب بالذات، فذلك الاحتمال أيضاً، قائم، لأن فيضان الآثار الحادثة عن ذلك المبدأ القديم، لابد وأن يكون موقوفاً على حصول شكل فلكي خاص. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إنه حصل شكل فلكي عجيب غريب، اقتضي فناء ذلك الشخص الأول في تلك اللحظة اللطيفة، وحصل شيء آخر يماثله دفعة؟ وأما القسم الثالث: وهو أن يقال: مؤثر العالم فاعل مختار. فعلى هذا التقدير: القول بأنه أفنى الشخص الأول في تلك اللحظة اللطيفة، وأوجد شخصاً آخر يماثله من كل الوجوه: محتمل. فثبت بهذا البرهان القاطع: أن تجويز هذا المعنى قائم على جميع التقديرات.
Firstly, while the definitive possibility stands as being different from that first [matter]. Its explanation: that the contingent event either does not need an effect or needs it, and the second division: the effect of the world is either necessitating or free. It is apparent that there is no division beyond these three divisions at all. As for the first division: that it is said: there is no effect for these events. On this estimation, existence after non-existence and non-existence after existence occur by mere coincidence without any cause. And if that is possible, then it is also possible to say: it happened that that first person ceased to exist in that subtle moment, and the second person came to be by coincidence. Because the claim of coincidence, since it occurs in some cases, its possibility stands in all cases. As for the second division: that it is said: the effect of the world is a necessitating cause by essence, then that possibility also stands, because the emanation of the originated effects from that eternal principle must be dependent on the occurrence of a particular celestial configuration. And if this is established, we say: why is it not permissible to say that a strange and wondrous celestial configuration occurred, necessitating the annihilation of that first person in that subtle moment, and another thing similar to it came to be instantly? As for the third division: that it is said: the effect of the world is a free agent. On this estimation: the statement that He annihilated the first person in that subtle moment and brought into existence another person similar to him in all respects is possible. So it is established by this definitive proof that the possibility of this meaning stands on all estimations.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents a threefold classification of possible causes for contingent events and argues that in each case, the possibility of replacing one person with another identical one at a precise moment remains. The author first distinguishes between events that require a cause and those that do not, but dismisses the latter as impossible because he is assuming contingency. He then divides the cause into two types: necessitating (such that the effect follows necessarily) and free (such that the agent has choice). He examines each of the three positions (no cause, necessitating cause, free cause) and shows that in all three, it is conceivable that a person could be annihilated and replaced by an exactly similar person. The first position (no cause) leads to pure coincidence, so the substitution is possible by chance. The second (necessitating cause) requires specific celestial conditions, which could be arranged to cause the substitution. The third (free cause) allows the agent to directly annihilate and create. Thus the proof concludes that the theoretical possibility of such substitution is established on all three views.

الاتفاق

Misconception: Some may think that only a free agent can cause substitution, but the author shows it is possible under all three causal theories.
Correction: The passage demonstrates that substitution is conceivable even under chance and necessitating cause.

B) It is possible only in the first scenario.

Substitution of identical persons is rationally possible under all causal theories.

The Mechanism of Dream Images: Sleep and the Common Sense
Describes how dream images form when external senses are suspended.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the absence of external senses during sleep allows the imaginative faculty to imprint images on the common sense, explaining the realism of dreams.

Core Idea
In sleep, the empty common sense becomes a canvas for the imaginative faculty.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah explains the psychological process behind dream formation. It identifies two obstacles that normally prevent the imaginative faculty from projecting its creations onto the common sense: active external senses and the soul's preoccupation. During sleep, the first obstacle is removed, revealing a foundational mechanism for all similar states.

Guided Path
1
Understand the key concepts
Familiarize yourself with 'common sense' (الحس المشترك) as the faculty that perceives unified sensations, and 'imaginative faculty' (المتخيلة) as the power that composes and manipulates images.
These are central to the explanation.
2
Read the general condition
The passage begins with a general principle: when one or both obstacles are removed, the imaginative faculty can indicate or form images in the common sense.
3
Focus on sleep
During sleep, the external senses are inactive, so no external images enter the common sense. This leaves the common sense empty and receptive.
4
Observe the process of image formation
The imaginative faculty composes images and sends them down to the common sense. Because no external images compete, these internal images are perceived as if they were real.
5
Connect to dream realism
Reflect on how this explains why dreams often feel vivid and real: the common sense is fooled into treating internal images as external sensations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا، فنقول: إنه إذا انتفى الشاغلان معاً، أو أحدهما، فإنه يحصل ذلك التلويح، وذلك التشبيح. أما في وقت النوم فقد زال أحد الشاغلين، وهو الحس الظاهر، فلا ينتقل من الحواس الظاهرة إلى الحس المشترك شيء من الصور، فيبقى لوح الحس المشترك خالياً عن النقوش الخارجية، فيستعد لقبول الصور التي تركبها المتخيلة فتنحدر تلك الصور من المتخيلة إلى لوح الحس المشترك. فتصير محسوسة. وأما في وقت المرض، فإن النفس تصير مشغولة بتدبير البدن، فلا تتفرغ لمنع القوة المتخيلة من تركيب تلك الصور، فحينئذ تقوى المتخيلة على عملها. وإذا قويت على هذا العمل عصت الحس المشترك عن قبول الصور الخارجية، فوردت عليه هذه الصور فتصير مشاهدة محسوسة والصور الهائلة التي نصير مشاهدة في حالة الخوف. هي من هذا الباب. فإن الخوف المستولي على النفس يصدها عن تأديب المتخيلة فلا جرم تقدر المتخيلة على رسم صورها في الحس المشترك كصورة الغول وغيرها. وكذلك قد تستولي على النفس الضعيفة العقل: قوى أخرى كشهوة شيء فتشتد تلك الشهرة، حتى تغلب العقل. فالمتخيلة تركب صورة ذلك المشتهى، فتتطبع تلك الصورة في لوح الحس المشترك، فتصير محسوسة.
If you know this, we say: When both obstacles are absent, or one of them, then that indication and that formation occur. As for during sleep, one of the obstacles has been removed, which is the external sense, so nothing of the forms is transferred from the external senses to the common sense; thus the tablet of the common sense remains empty of external impressions, and it becomes ready to accept the forms that the imaginative faculty composes. Then those forms descend from the imaginative faculty to the tablet of the common sense, and they become perceived. As for during illness, the soul becomes preoccupied with managing the body, so it does not have leisure to prevent the imaginative power from composing those forms; then the imaginative faculty gains strength for its work. When it gains strength for this work, it prevents the common sense from accepting external forms, so these forms come upon it and become visible and perceived. And the terrifying forms that we see in a state of fear are of this category. For the fear that dominates the soul prevents it from disciplining the imaginative faculty; inevitably the imaginative faculty is able to depict its forms in the common sense, such as the form of the ghoul and others. Likewise, other forces may take control of the weak-minded soul, such as the desire for something, and that desire becomes intense until it overcomes reason. Then the imaginative faculty composes the image of that desired thing, and that image is impressed upon the tablet of the common sense, and it becomes perceived.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage explains the philosophical mechanism behind visions during sleep and illness. It begins by stating that when one or both of the two obstacles (the external senses and the soul's preoccupation) are removed, the imaginative faculty can impress its compositions onto the common sense, making them seem real. During sleep, the external senses are suspended, so no external forms enter the common sense, leaving it empty and receptive to the imaginative faculty's compositions. During illness, the soul is busy managing the body, so it cannot restrain the imaginative faculty from composing forms; thus the imaginative faculty overpowers the common sense and impresses its images. The passage also notes that terrifying images seen in fear, such as that of a ghoul, occur because fear occupies the soul and prevents it from disciplining the imaginative faculty. Similarly, strong desire can overcome reason, allowing the imaginative faculty to picture the desired object in the common sense. This explanation is set as a foundation for discussing dreams and visions.

المتخيلة

الصور

Correction: This passage shows dreams have a natural psychological mechanism: the removal of external sense input allows the imaginative faculty to project its own compositions onto the common sense.

Key Takeaway
During sleep, the removal of external sense input allows the imaginative faculty to project its own compositions onto the common sense, creating dream images.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have woken from countless dreams—have you ever paused to recognize that the vivid scenes you experienced were entirely constructed by your own imagination? This lesson exposes the mechanism: your common sense, stripped of outer input, became a canvas for your inner faculty. How does that change your awareness of what you perceive as 'real'? Will you now pay closer attention to the difference between external and internal sources of perception?
?
The passage describes the removal of 'one of the obstacles'—the external senses—during sleep. But what about the other obstacle: the soul's preoccupation? In your waking life, are you so entangled with worldly concerns that your inner imaginative faculty is silenced? Could it be that your neglect of inner reflection is a choice to keep that obstacle in place? What would it cost you to intentionally quiet your external senses and listen to what arises from within?
?
When you remember a dream, have you ever considered that the images were 'descended' from your imaginative faculty to your common sense, making them 'perceived' as if from outside? If your own mind can produce such convincing illusions in sleep, what does that say about your certainty in waking perceptions? Are you living as if everything you sense is absolutely true, or are you open to the possibility that your inner faculties shape your reality more than you admit? What step will you take today to reflect on the nature of your own consciousness?

The Mechanism of Visions in Illness, Fear, and Desire
Explains how illness, fear, and desire lead to vivid images.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how illness, fear, and intense desire enable the imaginative faculty to override the common sense and produce vivid images.

Core Idea
When the soul is preoccupied, the imaginative faculty's images become real.

Continuing the explanation, the passage now turns to states where the soul is preoccupied—illness, fear, and desire. In these states, the second obstacle (the soul's engagement) is removed, allowing the imaginative faculty to dominate and impress its forms onto the common sense. This explains hallucinations and intense mental images.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two obstacles
Remember that the two obstacles are active external senses and the soul's preoccupation. In sleep, we saw the first removed. Now we look at the second.
2
Read about illness
During illness, the soul is busy managing the body, so it cannot restrain the imaginative faculty. This allows the imagination to gain strength and block external forms from entering the common sense.
The soul's preoccupation is a key factor.
3
Understand the role of fear
Fear dominates the soul, preventing it from disciplining the imaginative faculty. Thus, the imagination can depict terrifying forms like ghouls in the common sense.
4
Examine the case of desire
Intense desire overcomes reason, allowing the imaginative faculty to compose and impress the image of the desired object onto the common sense, making it seem present.
5
Reflect on the common mechanism
In all these cases, the soul's preoccupation or weakness leaves the imaginative faculty unchecked, so it can project its compositions onto the common sense, which then perceives them as real.

Causality and Created Things
beginner

The Sophistical Consequence of Allowing Imaginary Perceptions
Refutation showing opponent's position leads to sophistry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that the opponent's position leads to universal doubt (sophistry) while his own view does not.

Core Idea
Allowing any perception to be imaginary makes all perceptions doubtful.

The author begins by turning the tables: the very objection the opponent raises against his view actually boomerangs back more strongly. He shows that if we allow that some perceived images might be non-existent, then we must also doubt every sensory experience—people, mountains, thunder—which is pure sophistry. In contrast, his own view only acknowledges that some things may go unperceived, not that perceived things are unreal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author sets up a comparison between two positions.
The passage is the first part of the refutation, ending at the conclusion that the opponent's statement is extremely corrupt.
2
Understand the boomerang argument
The author says the opponent's objection actually applies more severely to them. Identify why allowing one imaginary image logically extends to all images.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Contrast the two positions
The author contrasts his certainty about perceived things with the opponent's doubt. Note the key point: his view only allows non-perception, not non-existence of perceived things.
4
Reflect on the outcome
The passage concludes that the opponent's position leads to complete ignorance and sophistry. Consider why this is more damaging than the initial objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نسمعها ، ذلك يوجب الجهالات [ فيقال لهم : هذه الجهالات التي ألزمتموها على هذا القول، فهي على قولكم ألزم . وذلك لأنا لو جوزنا أن يرى الإنسان صوراً، ويشاهدها، ويتكلم معها، ويسمع أصواتها، ويرى أشكالها . ثم إنها لا تكون موجودة في الخارج، جاز أيضاً في كل هذه الأشياء التي نراها ونسمعها من صور الناس والجبال والبحار وأصوات الرعود، أن لا يكون لشيء منها وجود في الخارج بل يكون محض الخيال ومحض الصور المرتسمة في الحس المشترك . ومعلوم أن القول به محض السفسطة بل نقول : هذا في البعد عن الحق، والخوض في الجهالة أشد من الأول . لأن على القول الذي نقول نحن به، جازمون بأن كل ما رأيناه فهو موجود حق، إلا أنه يلزمنا تجويز أن يكون قد حضر عندنا أشياء، ونحن ما رأيناها، وتجويز هذا لا يوجب الشك في وجود كل ما رأيناه وسمعناه، أما على القول الذي يقولونه فإنه يلزم وقوع الشك في وجود كل صورة رأيناها، وكل صوت سمعناه، وذلك هو الجهالة التامة، والسفسطة الكاملة . فثبت أن القول الذي اخترتموه في غاية الفساد. فإن قالوا : إن حصول هذه الحالة مشروطة بحصول أحوال منها : أن يكون الإنسان نائماً . ومنها : أن يكون ضعيف العقل، كما في حق المجانين . ومنها : أن يكون كامل النفس، قوي العقل . كما في حق الأنبياء والأولياء، فإذا لم يحصل شيء من هذه الأحوال . وكان الإنسان باقياً على مقتضى المزاج المعتدل، لم يحصل شيء من هذه الأحوال، فحينئذ يحصل القطع بوجود هذه الأشياء في الخارج فنقول في الجواب : إن بالطريق الذي ذكرتم، ظهر أنه لا يحس الإنسان بوجود صور، مع أنها لا تكون موجودة أصلاً . وإذا ظهر جواز هذا المعنى فنحن إنما يمكننا انتفاء هذه الحالة، إذا دللنا على أن الأسباب الموجبة لحصول هذه الحالة : محصورة في كذا وكذا. ونقيم على هذا الحصر برهاناً يقينياً . ثم نبين في هذا المقام الثاني أنها بأسرها منقضية زائلة بالبرهان اليقيني . ثم نبين في هذا المقام الثالث أن الحكم حال بقائه لا يستغني عن السبب . فإن بتقدير أن يكون الأمر كذلك لم يلزم من زوال تلك الأسباب زوال هذه الحالة . ثم على تقدير إقامة البراهين الجازمة على صحة هذه المقدمات، يصير جزمنا بحصول هذه الأشياء المحسوسات في الخارج موقوفاً على إثبات هذه المقدمات النظرية الغامضة، والموقوف على النظري الغامض، أولى أن يكون نظرياً غامضاً وحينئذ تبطل هذه العلوم المستفادة من الحس بطلاناً كلياً . فثبت : أن القول الذي ذكروه : قول باطل يوجب التزام السفسطة واعلم : أن الذي حمل هؤلاء الفلاسفة على ذكر هذه العلل والأسباب : إطباقهم على إنكار الملائكة ، وعلى إنكار الجن وقد بينا في كتاب الأرواح : أنه ليس لهم فيه شبهة ، ولا خيال يدل على نفي هذه الأشياء . وإذا كان أصل هذه الأقوال نفي الملائكة والجن وقد عرفت أنه ليس لهم فيه دليل . وفيه ما يوجب القول بالسفسطة، كان هذا القول في غاية الفساد والبطلان فهذا تمام الكلام في هذا الأصل
We hear that, that necessitates ignorances. So it is said to them: These ignorances which you have imposed upon this statement, then upon your statement they are more incumbent. That is because if we allow that a person may see images, and observe them, and speak with them, and hear their voices, and see their shapes, and then they are not existent in the external world, then it is also allowed in all these things that we see and hear, like images of people, mountains, seas, and sounds of thunder, that none of them have existence in the external world, but rather they are pure imagination and pure forms imprinted in the common sense. And it is known that holding this is pure sophistry. Rather we say: This is further from truth and deeper into ignorance than the first. Because according to the statement which we hold, we are certain that everything we have seen is existent as truth, except that it necessitates us to allow that things may have been present with us while we did not see them, and allowing this does not necessitate doubt in the existence of everything we have seen and heard. As for the statement which they say, it necessitates the occurrence of doubt in the existence of every image we have seen and every sound we have heard, and that is complete ignorance and perfect sophistry. So it is established that the statement you have chosen is extremely corrupt. If they say: The occurrence of this state is conditional upon the occurrence of conditions: among them that the person is asleep; among them that he is weak in intellect, as in the case of madmen; among them that he is perfect in soul, strong in intellect, as in the case of prophets and saints. So if none of these conditions occurs, and the person remains according to the requirement of balanced temperament, none of these states occur, and then certainty of the existence of these things in the external world occurs. Then we say in response: By the method you have mentioned, it is shown that a person does not perceive the existence of images while they are not existent at all. And if the permissibility of this meaning is shown, then we can only negate this state if we demonstrate that the causes necessitating the occurrence of this state are restricted to such and such. And we establish a certain proof for this restriction. Then we show in this second station that they are all passing away and vanishing by certain proof. Then we show in this third station that the judgment during its continuance does not dispense with the cause. For if the matter were so, it would not follow from the removal of those causes the removal of this state. Then if we suppose the establishment of decisive proofs for the correctness of these premises, our certainty of the occurrence of these sensible things in the external world becomes dependent upon establishing these obscure theoretical premises. And that which depends on the obscure theoretical is more fitting to be obscure theoretical. Then these sciences obtained from sense become null and void completely. So it is established that the statement they mentioned is a false statement necessitating the adoption of sophistry. And know: That which drove these philosophers to mention these causes and reasons is their consensus on denying the angels and denying the jinn. And we have shown in the book 'Al-Arwah' that they have no doubt about it, nor any imagination indicating the negation of these things. And if the basis of these statements is the negation of angels and jinn, and you have known that they have no proof for it, and it contains what necessitates the statement of sophistry, then this statement is in extreme corruption and falsehood. So this is the completion of the speech on this principle.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a philosophical position that claims that what we perceive might be mere imagination and not existent in the external world. The author begins by stating that the objection posed against his own position actually applies more severely to the opponent's view. He argues that if we allow that a person can see, speak with, hear, and observe images that do not exist externally, then by the same logic everything we perceive (people, mountains, seas, thunder) could be mere imagination imprinted in the common sense, which is pure sophistry. He then compares the two positions: the author's view holds that everything we see is real, but it is possible that some things are present without being perceived, which does not create doubt in what we have perceived. The opponent's view, however, necessitates doubt in every perceived image and sound, leading to complete ignorance and sophistry. The author then presents an objection from the opponent: they claim that such imaginary states only occur under specific conditions—sleep, weak intellect, or perfect intellect (as in prophets and saints). Without these conditions, we are certain of external existence. The author answers by pointing out that the opponent's reasoning itself shows that a person can perceive non-existent images. To rule out this possibility, one would need to prove that the causes of such states are limited and that they are all absent, and that the judgment of existence continues only while the cause persists. This would require establishing obscure theoretical premises, which would make our certainty of sense-perception dependent on theoretical reasoning, thus undermining the immediate certainty of sense perception. Therefore, the opponent's position ultimately leads to sophistry. Finally, the author notes that the real motive behind these philosophers' arguments is their denial of angels and jinn, and since they have no proof for that, and their position entails sophistry, it is utterly corrupt.

Key Terms
جهالات
محض الخيال
الحس المشترك
السفسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author's own view leads to doubt in perception.
Correction: The author's view only allows that some real things may go unperceived, not that perceived things are unreal. It does not create doubt about what has been perceived.

Checkpoint
What does the author claim is the consequence of the opponent's position?
A) It leads to the denial of angels only.
B) It leads to doubt in all perceptions, i.e., sophistry.
C) It supports the certainty of sense perception.
D) It proves the existence of the external world.

Key Takeaway
Allowing any perception to be imaginary forces us to doubt all perceptions, which is pure sophistry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you doubted a clear perception—like a sound or a sight—because someone told you it might be an illusion? How does this passage expose the danger of allowing that doubt to spread to all your perceptions? What would you say to that doubter now?
?
Are there truths about Allah’s signs in creation that you have treated as 'imaginary'—acknowledging them in theory but not letting them shape your certainty and action? What would it take for you to treat them as real, as the author insists you must?

Refuting the Conditionality Defense and Exposing Hidden Motives
Refutation of the conditional defense and exposure of underlying denial of spirits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the opponent's attempt to limit imaginary perceptions to special conditions, and why he traces the root of the view to denial of angels and jinn.

Core Idea
The opponent's conditional defense collapses because it requires theoretical proof that undermines sense certainty.

The opponent tries to salvage their position by claiming that imaginary perceptions only occur under specific conditions—sleep, madness, or prophetic states. The author responds by showing that even if such conditions exist, proving their absence requires elaborate theoretical reasoning, which makes our certainty of sense perception dependent on obscure theory—thereby destroying self-evident knowledge. He then reveals the real motive behind the philosophical position: the denial of angels and jinn, which has no evidence and leads to sophistry.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the passage from 'If they say...' to the end. Note the opponent's claim that imaginary perceptions are conditional, and the author's multi-step rebuttal.
The author presents three 'stations' (maqamat) in his response.
2
Analyze the three stations
The author argues that to negate the possibility of imaginary perceptions, one must (1) prove the causes are limited, (2) prove those causes are absent, and (3) show that the judgment of existence does not persist after the cause ceases. Each requires theoretical proof.
These are the three necessary demonstations.
3
Understand the dependency problem
If certainty of sense perception depends on these theoretical proofs, then sense perception becomes theoretical and loses its immediate certainty. That is self-defeating.
4
Consider the hidden motive
The author claims the philosophers' real reason for this theory is their denial of angels and jinn. He asserts this has no proof and leads to sophistry, so the whole position is corrupt.

Critique of the Psychic Explanation of Miracles
Razi narrates and critiques the philosophers' psychic explanation of miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's critique that the philosophers' certainty in attributing miracles solely to a psychic faculty is an arbitrary preference (tarjih bila murajjih) because many other explanations are equally possible.

Core Idea
Certainty without evidence is arbitrary preference.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's critique of the philosophers' theory that miracles are produced by a powerful imaginative faculty. After summarizing their view and its supporting evidence, Razi argues that this is just one hypothesis among many, making their certainty unjustified. The lesson will guide you through their claim, their evidence, and the decisive logical objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' claim
Read the first part of the passage where Razi summarizes the philosophers' view: miracles are possible because the imaginative faculty can affect bodies, and a perfectly strong soul can dispose of matter as it wishes.
Note the key terms: القوة الوهمية and القوة النفسانية.
2
Examine their evidence
Look at the evidence they offer: weak souls combined can produce effects (e.g., group prayer), so a strong soul alone can produce wonders.
Consider whether this analogy is convincing.
3
Understand the critique
Razi points out that this explanation is only one possibility among many—angels, jinn, celestial bodies, etc. could also be causes. Therefore, singling out the psychic faculty is arbitrary.
This is the core of the critique: الترجيح بلا مرجح (preference without a preferrer).
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi concludes that the philosophers' certainty is unfounded. The chapter ends with a transition to the topic of magic to distinguish miracles from magic.
Think about why distinguishing miracles from magic is important.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السابع في حكاية قول الفلاسفة في السبب الذي لأجله يقدر الأنبياء والأولياء على الاتيان بالمعجزات والكرامات

حاصل كلامهم فيه : أنا بينا في كتاب ( النفس ) : أن القوة الوهمية التي للإنسان ، قد تكون قادرة على التأثير في الأجسام. وذكرنا الوجوه الكثيرة في تقرير هذا الباب . وعند هذا قالوا : لا يمتنع وجود إنسان تكون نفسه كاملة في هذه القوة ، فلا جرم يقدر على التصرف في هيولى هذا العالم كيف شاء وأراد . ولما يقوي ذلك ؛ أن النفوس الضعيفة إذا اجتمعت فقد يحصل لها نوع من القوة المؤثرة . مثل : الجمع العظيم إذا اجتمعوا على توجيه الفكر إلى شيء معين ، ومثل ما يشاهد في صلاة الاستسقاء وغيرها . وإذا كان هذا محسوسا لم يمتنع كون النفس القوية قادرة على الإتيان بهذه الغرائب والبدائع واعلم أن حاصل هذا الكلام : أن تلك النفس موصوفة بخاصية ، لأجلها قدرت على الإتيان بهذه المعجزات والكرامات. وكما أن هذا محتمل ، فكذلك سائر الوجوه محتملة . مثل هذه الأحوال إلى الملائكة أو الجن ، أو الاتصالات الفلكية ، أو أفعال الكواكب ، التي هي أحياء ناطقة أو العقول والنفوس . وإذا كان الكل محتملاً ، كان جزمهم بإسناد هذه المعجزات إلى القوة النفسانية فقط . ترجيحاً من غير مرجح . وهذا آخر الكلام في هذا الباب . ويتلوه الكلام في السحر . ليحصل الفرق بين المعجز ، والسحر ، والنبي ، والساحر والله اعلم
Chapter Seven: On Narrating the Statement of the Philosophers concerning the Cause by which Prophets and Saints are able to bring Miracles and Charisms.

The gist of their statement therein: We have explained in the book 'The Soul' that the imaginative faculty (al-quwwa al-wahmiyya) which belongs to man may be capable of affecting bodies. And we mentioned many aspects in establishing this topic. Upon this they said: It is not impossible that there exist a human being whose soul is perfect in this faculty, so inevitably he is able to dispose of the matter (hayula) of this world however he wishes and desires. And what strengthens that is that weak souls when they gather may obtain a kind of effective power. For example: a great multitude when they assemble to direct thought toward a specific thing, and like what is observed in the prayer for rain (salat al-istisqa') and other things. And if this is perceptible, it is not impossible that the strong soul be capable of bringing about these wonders and marvels. And know that the gist of this speech is that that soul is described with a property on account of which it is empowered to bring these miracles and charisms. And just as this is possible, so too the other aspects are possible: attributing these states to angels or jinn, or celestial conjunctions, or actions of the planets which are rational living beings, or to intellects and souls. And if all are possible, then their certainty in attributing these miracles solely to the psychic faculty (al-quwwa al-nafsaniyya) is a preference without a preferrer (tarjih bila murajjih). And this is the end of the discussion in this chapter. It will be followed by the discussion on magic (sihr) in order that the difference between the miracle (mu'jiz), magic, the prophet, and the sorcerer be obtained. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a chapter that narrates the philosophers' explanation of miracles and then critiques it. The author first states the philosophers' claim: they have established in their book on the soul that the imaginative faculty (al-quwwa al-wahmiyya) can affect bodies, and it is possible for a human soul to be perfect in this power, allowing its possessor to dispose of matter (hayula) at will. They support this by noting that even weak souls combined can produce effects, as in group prayer (salat al-istisqa'). Then the author, likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, points out that this claim is only one possibility among many. Attributing miracles to angels, jinn, celestial conjunctions, planetary actions, intellects, or souls is equally possible. Therefore, the philosophers' certainty in attributing miracles solely to the psychic faculty (al-quwwa al-nafsaniyya) constitutes an arbitrary preference (tarjih bila murajjih), which is rationally unjustified. The chapter ends with a transition to the topic of magic (sihr) to distinguish between miracles, magic, prophets, and sorcerers.

Key Terms
القوة الوهمية
المعجزات
الكرامات
الترجيح بلا مرجح
القوة النفسانية
السحر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' psychic explanation is the only rational account of miracles.
Correction: Razi shows it is just one possibility among many (angels, jinn, celestial bodies, etc.), so certainty in it is arbitrary.

Checkpoint
What is the main critique Razi levels against the philosophers' explanation?
A) It contradicts the Quran.
B) It is only one possibility among many, so their certainty is arbitrary.
C) It denies the existence of angels.
D) It relies on a false analogy.

Key Takeaway
Razi's critique shows that the philosophers' psychic explanation of miracles is merely one hypothesis among many, making their certainty an arbitrary preference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read Razi's critique: the philosophers were certain without sufficient evidence. Where in your own life have you held a belief with certainty simply because it was familiar, without considering other possibilities? Can you today identify one belief you hold about your faith or life that you have not critically examined, and commit to investigating it with the same scrutiny Razi applies?
?
Razi shows that attributing miracles to the soul's power is just one possibility. How often do you attribute the good things in your life—your skills, achievements, even your faith—to your own abilities, forgetting that Allah could be working through angels, circumstances, or His direct will? What would change if you consciously attributed everything to Allah first?
?
The philosophers relied on group effects like prayer for rain to support their view. When you see collective worship or large numbers of Muslims praying, do you feel a sense of spiritual power? How can you ensure that your reliance is on Allah's mercy rather than on the human energy of the crowd?

The First Type of Magic: Astral Magic
The author describes the first and strongest type of magic based on astral judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the causal theory behind astral magic as described by the author, including its philosophical and experimental foundations and the mechanism of agent and recipient.

Core Idea
Astral magic is a systematic cause-effect process based on celestial influences and empirical correspondences.

The author begins his typology of magic by presenting the first and strongest type: astral magic. He explains that this form of magic is not arbitrary but rests on a philosophical and experimental framework. In this passage, he lays out the foundational premises and the practical method that supposedly guarantees its effects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the flow from definition to proof to method.
Notice the phrase '— as it is said —' which may indicate the author is reporting a view rather than endorsing it.
2
Identify the components
Break the passage into parts: the definition of astral magic, the philosophical proof, the experimental reinforcement, the specific planetary correspondences, and the practical method.
The passage transitions from theoretical to empirical to practical.
3
Understand the agent-recipient model
Focus on the final part: the strong agent (al-fāʿil al-qawī) and the receptive materials (al-mawādd al-qābilah). The author claims that when these are combined, the effect must appear.
Consider how this causal necessity relates to the philosophical and experimental premises.
4
Reflect on the author's stance
Consider that the author presents this explanation as what is said ('as it is said'). In the broader context, he may be laying out the view to critique it later.
This passage is descriptive; the author's own evaluation comes later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة
في بيان أنواع السمر
النوع الأول : وهو أعظمها قوة ، وأشدها تأثيراً ـ على ما يقال ـ السحر المبني على مقتضيات أحكام النجوم .
وتقرير الكلام فيه : أنه ثبت بالدلائل الفلسفية : أن مباديء حدوث الحوادث في هذا العالم ، هو الأشكال الفلكية ، والاتصالات الكوكبية . ثم إن التجارب المعتبرة في علم الأحكام ، انضافت إلى تلك الدلائل ، فقويت تلك المقدمة جداً . ثم إن التجارب النجومية دلت على اختصاص كل واحد من هذه الكواكب السيارة بأشياء معينة من هذا العالم الأسفل ، فلكل واحد منها طعوم مخصوصة ، وروائح مخصوصة [ وأشكال مخصوصة (۱) ] ومن المعادن كذا ، ومن النبات كذا ، ومن الحيوان كذا .
فإذا طلب من الكوكب حالة مخصوصة ، مناسبة لعمل مخصوص ثم جمع بين الأشياء الفعلية (۲) المناسبة لذلك الكوكب ، ولذلك الأثر ، فحينئذ قد حصل الفاعل القوي على ذلك الفعل ، وحصلت المواد القابلة لذلك الأثر المناسبة له . وعند الجمع بين الفاعل وبين القابل ، لابد وأن يظهر الأثر . فهذا شرح هذا النوع من السحر
Preface on explaining the types of magic. The first type: and it is the greatest of them in strength, and the most severe in effect — as it is said — the magic built upon the requirements of astral judgments. And the explanation of the speech concerning it: that it is established by philosophical proofs that the principles of the occurrence of events in this world are the celestial configurations and the planetary conjunctions. Then reliable experiments in the science of judgments were added to those proofs, so that premise became very strong. Then astral experiments indicated the specialization of each one of these wandering planets to specific things from this lower world, so for each one of them there are specific tastes, specific smells, [and specific shapes (1)], and from minerals such-and-such, from plants such-and-such, from animals such-and-such. So when a specific state is sought from the planet, appropriate for a specific work, then one gathers the actual things (2) appropriate to that planet and that effect, then at that time the strong agent has been obtained for that act, and the receptive materials for that effect appropriate to it have been obtained. And upon the combination of the agent and the recipient, the effect must appear. So this is the explanation of this type of magic.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage classifies and explains the first type of magic (al-siḥr), which the author describes as the most powerful and effective. The author begins by stating that this type is astral magic, based on the dictates of astrological judgments. He then presents its theoretical foundation: philosophical proofs establish that the origins (mabādiʾ) of events in this world are celestial configurations (al-ashkāl al-falakiyyah) and planetary conjunctions (al-ittiṣālāt al-kawkabiyyah). Empirical experiments in the science of astral judgments (ʿilm al-aḥkām) reinforce these proofs, making the premise very strong. Furthermore, astral experiments show that each of the wandering planets (al-kawākib al-sayyārah) has specific associations with objects in the sublunar world: each has particular tastes, smells, shapes, and corresponding minerals, plants, and animals. The practical method follows: to obtain a desired effect from a planet, one must gather the appropriate active elements (al-ashyāʾ al-fiʿliyyah) that correspond to that planet and the intended effect. When the strong agent (al-fāʿil al-qawī) and the receptive materials (al-mawādd al-qābilah) are combined, the effect must necessarily appear. The author concludes that this is the explanation of this type of magic. The passage presents a cause-and-effect mechanism based on astral influences, which will presumably be critiqued or refuted in the broader context of the work.

Definition of Talisman and the Rational Proof for Its Possibility
Definition of talisman and beginning of rational proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Philosophers and Sabians define a talisman and the first steps of their rational proof for its possibility.

Core Idea
A talisman is a blend of celestial and earthly forces to produce unusual effects.

This lesson introduces the concept of talisman as understood by the Philosophers and Sabians. It begins with a precise definition, then outlines a rational proof that attempts to establish the causal framework necessary for talismans to work. Although the proof is not completed here, the foundational reasoning is presented.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the key terms: talisman, active celestial forces, passive terrestrial forces.
The definition is the first sentence.
2
Identify the two components
Notice that the definition involves a mixture of two types of forces: active celestial and passive terrestrial.
3
Understand the purpose
The purpose is to produce something contrary to custom or prevent something that accords with custom.
4
Follow the rational proof's first premises
The proof starts with the existence of events and the principle that every event has a cause. Then it considers the cause's nature.
5
Observe the incomplete argument
The text breaks off at 'infinite regress is of two divisions'. This indicates the argument continues elsewhere.
The lesson covers only the beginning.

This passage opens the first chapter of the work, which is devoted to the topic of God. It begins by presenting the definition of talisman according to the Philosophers and the Sabians. The argument seems to be leading towards the conclusion that the ultimate cause must be eternal, which would then support the existence of active celestial forces necessary for talismans. The Rational Proof Against Infinite Regress
A rational proof distinguishing two types of regress in causal chains.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that rejects simultaneous infinite regress while accepting sequential regress, leading to the necessity of an eternal cause.

Core Idea
Not all infinite regresses are impossible; only simultaneous regress is refuted.

The philosophers' argument against infinite regress is a cornerstone of rational theology. This passage presents the logical structure: events exist, they need causes, and this leads to a choice between an infinite chain or an eternal first cause. Understanding the distinction between two types of regress is key to grasping the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Get a sense of the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the terms 'hadith' (temporal) and 'qadim' (eternal).
2
Identify the key premise
The argument begins with the premise that every event has a cause. This is the foundation.
3
Notice the division of causes
The cause is divided into temporal or eternal. This is a crucial dichotomy.
4
Distinguish the two types of regress
Regress is split into simultaneous (impossible) and sequential without a first (accepted). Understand the difference.
Simultaneous means all causes and effects exist at once; sequential means a chain with no beginning.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument forces acceptance of an eternal cause because sequential regress does not solve the need for a first cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الحجة العقلية الصرفة . فتقريرها : أن نقول : لا شك في وجود حوادث تحدث في هذا العالم ، وكل حادث فله سبب فهذه الحوادث لها سبب وذلك السبب إما أن يكون حادثاً ، وإما أن يكون قديماً . أما القسم الأول : وهو أن القول بأن أسباب هذه الحوادث شيء حادث : فنقول : الكلام في ذلك الحادث، كما في الأول . وذلك يوجب التسلسل . إلا أن التسلسل على قسمين : أحدهما : أن تحصل جميع الأسباب ، والمسببات دفعة واحدة ، وقد بينا في برهان إثبات واجب الوجود لذاته : امتناعه . والثاني : أن يكون كل واحد منها مسبوقاً بغيره ، لا إلى أول ، وهذا هو الحق الذي لا محيد عنه .
As for the purely rational proof, its formulation is: We say: There is no doubt about the existence of events that occur in this world, and every event has a cause. So these events have a cause, and that cause is either an event or eternal. As for the first division, which is the claim that the causes of these events are something temporal: We say: The discussion of that temporal is like the first. That necessitates regress (tasalsul). But regress is of two types: One of them: that all causes and effects occur simultaneously, and we have shown in the proof of establishing the Necessary Existence in Itself its impossibility. And the second: that each of them is preceded by another, without a first, and this is the truth from which there is no escape.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents the rational proof against infinite regress. It begins by asserting the undeniable existence of events in the world, each requiring a cause. That cause must be either temporal (hadith) or eternal (qadim). If it is temporal, the same question applies to its own cause, leading to regress. The argument then distinguishes two types of regress: simultaneous (where all causes and effects exist at once) and sequential without a first cause. The first type has already been refuted in the proof for the Necessary Existent. The second type is acknowledged as inevitable, meaning that a chain of causes must go back indefinitely without a first temporal cause, which forces the acceptance of an eternal cause.

Key Terms
حادث
قديم
تسلسل
سبب
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument rejects all forms of infinite regress.
Correction: The argument explicitly distinguishes between simultaneous regress (impossible) and sequential regress without a first (accepted). Only simultaneous regress is refuted.

Checkpoint
According to the rational proof, which type of regress is considered impossible?
A) Sequential regress without a first cause
B) Simultaneous occurrence of all causes and effects
C) Both types of regress
D) Neither type of regress

Key Takeaway
The rational proof shows that while simultaneous infinite regress is impossible, sequential regress without a first cause is accepted, leading to the necessity of an eternal cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on causes every day—food, air, family. When you honestly trace back your own existence, do you stop at something temporal (like your parents) or do you acknowledge the Eternal Cause behind the chain? What change in your daily life would reflecting on this truth produce?
?
The proof accepts sequential regress without a first. In your own actions, do you act as if you are an uncaused originator, or do you recognize you are part of a chain dependent on God? Which mindset aligns with this truth, and what habit will you change to live it?
?
If simultaneous regress is impossible, then you cannot relate to God as one cause among many in a simultaneous system. Are you trying to fit God into your immediate plans as just another factor, or do you see Him as the sole Eternal Cause behind your entire sequential existence? What will you do today to realign your perspective?
